Classification of Fallacies: Verbal, Conceptual, Involuntary, and Intentional
Author distinguishes between verbal and conceptual errors, and between involuntary and intentional fallacies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify logical fallacies as verbal or conceptual, and as involuntary (sahw) or intentional (eristic or sophistical) based on the criteria in this passage.

Core Idea
Fallacies are not just verbal errors; they also arise from mistaken belief and intent.

In this passage, the author explains that error in reasoning is not confined to words or meanings—both can be sources of fallacy. He then distinguishes between involuntary errors (al-sahw) committed while seeking truth, and intentional fallacies: eristic (mushāghabī) which aims at causation without proper obligation, and sophistical (sūfisṭā'ī) which pretends to demonstrate knowledge. Understanding these categories equips you to diagnose flawed arguments in Islamic scholarly debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim about error and correctness
Notice that the author first denies that error is specific to words and correctness to meanings. He asserts that both can occur in either. This establishes that fallacies are not merely linguistic.
الغلط لا يكون مخصوصا بالاسم، والصواب لا يكون مخصوصا بالمعنى
2
Distinguish between verbal and conceptual fallacies
The author explains that one who errs from utterance also errs from belief in some way, but much error is from belief alone, with no lexical component. This separates fallacies into two domains.
كثيرًا من الغلط هو من جهة الاعتقاد؛ ولا مدخل للفظ فيها
3
Extract the three types of fallacy in syllogism
The passage identifies three kinds: involuntary (سهو) when truth is sought but inadvertently built on similar principles; eristic (مشاغبي) aiming at causality without obligation; and sophistical (سوفسطائي) aiming at showing judgment. These are distinguished by their purpose and resemblance.
السهو… المشاغبي… السوفسطائي
4
Connect the types to argument forms
Learn that eristic resembles dialectical but is not truly dialectical; sophistical resembles demonstrative but is not. This helps you recognize them in actual arguments.
إذا أشبه الكلام بالقياس الجدلي ولم يكن جدليا… مشاغبية وإذا أشبه بالحكمي… مغالطية

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة فالغلط لا يكون مخصوصا بالاسم، والصواب لا يكون مخصوصا بالمعنى أيضًا بل قد يكون كل واحد منهما في هذا وفي ذلك كذلك؛ فليس المضلل هو الذي عند المسموع. والحق هو الذي عند المفهوم؛ وإن ما يكون عند المفهوم جنسًا للأقاويل الصحيحة فإن الذي يغلط من جهة اللفظ هو أيضًا يغلط من جهة الاعتقاد بوجه ما. وكثيرًا من الغلط هو من جهة الاعتقاد؛ ولا مدخل للفظ فيها كالذي بالعرض. وبالجملة تلك السبعة المعنوية . ثمَّ من الغلط في القياس المغالطي ما يكون غلطا مع طلب الحق؛ وإنما وقع سهوًا. والسبب فيه أن قايسه يطلب أن لن يبني على المبادئ الخاصة وأن ينساق إلى الحق لكنه سهى وبنى على شبيهه بالمبادئ الخاصة لا عليها. ومنه ما يكون الغرض فيه العلية بغير الواجب وهو المشاغبي، ومنه ما يكون الغرض فيه إظهار الحكم وفصل البيان وهو السوفسطائي. والأولى أن يسمَّى طالب العلية كيف اتفق مشاغبيا، وأن يسمى المتظاهر بالمعرفة سوفسطائيا. وقد مضى أنه إذا أشبه الكلام بالقياس الجدلي ولم يكن جدليا بالحقيقة كان القياس مشاغبيًّا، وإذا أشبه بالحكمي ولم يكن حكميًّا كان القياس مغالطيّا.
In summary, error is not specific to the name, nor is correctness specific to the meaning, but rather each of them can be in this and that as well. So the one misled is not the one by what is heard; the truth is what is understood. And what is understood is the genus of correct statements, for the one who errs from the aspect of utterance also errs from the aspect of belief in some way. Much error is from the aspect of belief, with no entry for utterance in it, like that which is accidental. In summary, those are the seven conceptual [fallacies]. Then from error in the sophistical syllogism is what is error with seeking truth, but it occurred inadvertently. The reason is that one who makes it intends to build upon specific principles and be led to truth, but he inadvertently built upon something resembling the specific principles, not upon them. And from it is what has the purpose of causality without obligation, which is the eristic, and from it is what has the purpose of showing judgment and clarity of explanation, which is the sophistical. It is more appropriate that the seeker of causality in any way be called eristic, and the one pretending to knowledge be called sophistical. It has passed that if speech resembles dialectical syllogism and is not truly dialectical, the syllogism is eristic; and if it resembles demonstrative and is not demonstrative, the syllogism is sophistical.
Source pages p. 594

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of error in logic. The author states that error is not confined to words (الأسماء) nor is correctness confined to meaning (المعنى); both can occur in either. A person is not misled merely by what is heard but by what is understood. However, what is understood is the genus of correct statements. The one who errs from the verbal side also errs from the belief side in some way. Many errors are from the belief side with no lexical component, like those that are accidental. Then the author enumerates the seven conceptual fallacies. Then he distinguishes three types of fallacies in syllogism: (1) involuntary error (سهو) when one seeks truth but mistakenly builds on similar principles; (2) eristic (مشاغبي) which aims at causation without obligation; (3) sophistical (سوفسطائي) which aims at showing judgment and clarity. The author recommends calling the seeker of causation eristic, and the pretender to knowledge sophistical. He reiterates that if an argument resembles dialectical but is not, it is eristic; if it resembles demonstrative but is not, it is sophistical.

Fallacies are divided into verbal and conceptual, and into involuntary (sahw) and intentional (eristic and sophistical), which is crucial for diagnosing errors in Islamic intellectual discourse.

Practical Techniques for Using Fallacies in Debate
Author enumerates techniques for employing fallacies: prolongation, speed, emotional arousal, changing order, mixing arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify five fallacious techniques listed by the author (prolongation, speed, emotional bias, changing order, mixing arguments) and explain how each undermines rational argument in an Islamic debate setting.

Core Idea
Fallacies are weapons of sophistry; mastering their identification protects the seeker of truth.

This passage from a classical logic text goes beyond theory to show how fallacies are actually employed in debate. The author enumerates practical techniques — from prolonging speech to emotional arousal — that can be used either to deceive (sophistry) or to manage stubborn opponents. As part of adab al-bahth (the etiquette of debate), recognising these tactics is essential for any serious debater or student of Islamic intellectual discourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first technique: prolongation (al-taṭwīl)
The author states that prolongation mixes speech and distances parts of the statement, hiding the direction towards the desired conclusion. Notice how this prevents the listener from following the argument's logical flow.
2
Identify the second technique: speed (al-isti'māl)
Speed means speaking so quickly that the time of expression precedes the time for reflection. This prevents careful reasoning and analysis of the argument.
3
Identify the third technique: emotional bias through irritation (al-ta'aṣṣub bil-ta'ttub)
Emotional arousal overwhelms the power of thought and distracts from the evidence. Recognise how emotional manipulation can sideline rational deliberation.
The exact phrase may be corrupt but the concept is clear.
4
Identify the fourth and fifth techniques: changing order and mixing arguments
Changing the order and position hides the conclusion. Mixing one proof with another and one statement with another creates confusion and suggests that the desired conclusion follows when it may not. These techniques obscure the logical structure.
5
Summarise the overall purpose
The author concludes that all these techniques help prevent premises from being present in the mind, or if present, cause heedlessness of how the premises lead to the conclusion. Recognise that the goal is to disrupt the rational connection between evidence and claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في استعمال هذه المضللات. وهذه المضللات قد تستعمل للمغالطة، وقد تستعمل في مخاطبة العناد. ويعينها الترتيب الأنفع وحسن التصرف في استعمالها كما يعين الجدلية: فمن ذلك التطويل حتى يختلط الكلام وتتباعد أجزاء القول بعضها من بعض فیخفي توجهها إلى المطلوب. ومن ذلك الاستعمال حتى يسبق زمان العبارة زمان جودة التأمل والروية، ومن ذلك التعصب بالتعتب حتى يغلب الانفعال النفساني قوة الفكرة فيشغلها عن البينة. وجميع ذلك يعين على أن لا يحضر جميع المقدمات في الذهن؛ وإن حضرت غفل عن جهة تأديها إلى النتيجة. ومن ذلك أيضًا يغير الترتيب والوضع لإخفاء النتيجة، وكذلك خلط حجة بحجة، وقول بقول، وإبهام أنه تروّج إنتاج المطلوب. وبالجملة فالقوانين
Section: On the use of these fallacious techniques. These fallacious techniques may be used for sophistry, and may be used in addressing stubbornness. They are aided by the most beneficial arrangement and good management in their use, just as dialectical [techniques] are aided. Among that is prolongation until speech becomes mixed and parts of the statement become distant from each other, thus hiding their direction towards the desired [conclusion]. And among that is acceleration so that the time of expression precedes the time of good reflection and deliberation. And among that is emotional bias through irritation so that the psychological emotion overcomes the power of thought and distracts it from the evidence. All of that helps that all premises are not present in the mind; and if they are present, one is heedless of the way they lead to the result. And among that also is changing the order and position to hide the result, as well as mixing a proof with a proof, and a statement with a statement, and suggesting that the production of the desired [conclusion] is promoted. In summary, the laws...
Source pages p. 595

Commentary
This section (فصل) discusses the use of fallacious techniques (مضللات) both for pure sophistry and for dealing with stubborn opponents. The author states that these techniques are aided by effective arrangement and good management, just as dialectical techniques are. Then he lists specific methods: (1) Prolongation (تطويل) to confuse the speech and distance the parts of the argument so that its direction towards the desired conclusion is hidden. (2) Speed (استعمال) such that the time of expression precedes the time for proper reflection. (3) Emotional bias (تعصب بالتعتب) so that emotional response overwhelms the intellectual faculty and distracts it from the evidence. All these help prevent the premises from being present in the mind, or if present, the way they lead to the conclusion is forgotten. (4) Changing the order and arrangement to conceal the conclusion. (5) Mixing one proof with another and one statement with another, and insinuating that the desired conclusion is produced. The passage ends with 'وبالجملة فالقوانين' indicating a summary.

Distinguishing Absurdity from Falsehood in Sophistry
Author distinguishes between absurd (هذيان) and false (باطل) statements with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a statement is absurd (هذيان) or merely false (باطل) by checking for self-contradiction and informativeness.

Core Idea
Absurdity is self-contradictory; falsehood is coherent but mistaken.

In dealing with sophistical arguments, it is crucial to distinguish between mere falsehood and outright absurdity. The author of this passage warns that an absurd statement, like the example about 'double', undermines itself and provides no benefit to the listener. This lesson will teach you to spot the difference and respond appropriately.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms being defined
Find the Arabic terms هذيان (absurd) and باطل (false) in the passage. The author explicitly contrasts them. Note how the example is labeled 'هذا هذيان'.
2
Extract the distinguishing criterion
The author states: 'الهذيان غير الباطل' and that absurdity makes what follows from it absurd, not false. The key criterion is that an absurd statement is self-contradictory and yields no informative benefit once understood.
3
Apply the criterion to the example
Analyze the example: 'ليس الضعف ضعفًا للنصف فالنصف له ضعف ؛ فيكون الضعف إذن ضعف ما له ضعفين'. Why is this absurd? Because once you understand 'double', the statement becomes a tautology or contradiction, providing no new information.
The example uses a logical variable 'أ' (A) to represent a speaker.
4
Distinguish from falsehood
A false statement (e.g., 'the sky is green') is coherent but factually incorrect. An absurd statement is incoherent from the start. Practice by classifying other statements as absurd or false.
The shift_alert reminds us that later logicians sometimes treat هذيان as a subtype of falsehood, but here it is distinct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان مشاغبيا لم يكن ذلك منه بقصد. وإما أن يكون قاصدًا إلى المشاغبة قطعًا. وإن فهم الحق فكان له قدرة أن يفهم؛ فليس يتبع معه الاشتغال بتفهيم الحق فيجب أن يرمي عن قوته. وأما الذي لا يفهم القوانين؛ ولو فهمها فإما أن لا يحاور أصلا، وإما أن يكون لداع من الدعاوي وعلة من العلل: والأولى أن لا يشتغل معه بما لا تحدى. أو لا يفهم مثلا إذا قال قائل «أ» ليس الضعف ضعفًا للنصف فالنصف له ضعف ؛ فيكون الضعف إذن ضعف ما له ضعفين: وهذا هذيان فإذن ليس الضعف ضعفًا للنصف. وإنما وقع هذا لأنه لم يُعلم أن الهذيان غير الباطل. وإن الهذيان يجعل ما يجب عنه هذيانا مثله لا باطلا؛ فقوله الضعف ضعف النصف هذيان فإنه لا ضعف إلا ضعف النصف. فإذا فهمنا الضعف لم يكن لنا فائدة في أن يقول إنه ضعف النصف، وعلى هذا فيما يكون من مثل هذا الكلام.
If someone is a quarrelsome person and that was not his intent, or if he is intentionally quarrelsome, and he understood the truth and had the ability to understand, then occupying oneself with teaching him the truth is not followed; rather he must be repelled. As for one who does not understand the laws, even if he understood them, either he does not debate at all, or he has some claims and causes. It is better not to engage with him in what does not succeed. Or he does not understand, for example, when someone says: 'A' says: 'The double is not the double of half, so half has a double; therefore the double is the double of what has two doubles.' This is absurdity. Thus the double is not the double of half. This occurred because it was not known that the absurd is not the false. Indeed, the absurd makes that which is required from it an absurdity like it, not false. So his statement 'the double is the double of half' is absurd, for there is no double except the double of half. So if we understand 'double', we have no benefit in his saying it is the double of half. And this applies to similar speech.
Source pages p. 595

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes between two types of problematic statements in sophistical arguments: the absurd (هذيان) and the false (باطل). The author first discusses the character of a quarrelsome disputant (مشاغب), who may either unintentionally cause confusion or deliberately seek to argue. If he understands the truth and has ability, it is useless to teach him—he should be silenced. For one who does not grasp the logical laws, it is best to avoid debate. Then the author gives a concrete example: someone claims 'the double is not the double of half', leading to an absurd conclusion that the double is the double of what has two doubles. This is labeled as هذيان (absurdity), not merely باطل (false). The key point is that an absurd statement is inherently incoherent and self-refuting, whereas a false statement could be coherent but mistaken. The author emphasizes that once the meaning of 'double' is understood, the statement 'the double is the double of half' becomes tautological and useless. Thus, the passage trains the reader to identify when an opponent's speech has crossed from falsehood into pure absurdity, which requires a different response.

The Purposes and Techniques of Fallacies in Sophistry
Author lists purposes of fallacies and techniques for confusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two main purposes of fallacies (deception and rebutting stubbornness) and the four rhetorical techniques used to confuse the opponent (prolonging, haste, emotional distraction, mixing arguments).

Core Idea
Fallacies can hide truth as effectively as logic reveals it.

The author begins a chapter dedicated to the use of fallacies (المضللات) in disputation. He explains that fallacies are not only for deception but also for dealing with stubborn opponents. He then lists several techniques that exploit the opponent's cognitive limitations, such as speaking too quickly or mixing arguments. Understanding these techniques is essential for both employing and defending against fallacies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two purposes
Look for the statement 'قد تستعمل للمغالطة، وقد تستعمل في مخاطبة العناد' to see that fallacies have two distinct applications.
2
List the techniques mentioned
Find the phrases starting with 'فمن ذلك...': application, haste, fanaticism, changing order, and mixing arguments.
Each technique targets a different mental faculty.
3
Analyze how each technique prevents clear reasoning
For each technique, explain which cognitive process it disrupts (e.g., prolonging disrupts memory, haste prevents reflection, emotional reproach distracts reason).
The goal is to prevent premises from being present in the mind or to hide how they lead to the conclusion.
4
Consider the broader maxim
Note the statement 'فالقوانين المنطقية معتبرة من طرفي السائل والمجيب'—logical laws apply to both sides, so truth and falsehood can be manipulated through the same structures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : في استعمال هذه المضللات وهذه المضللات قد تستعمل للمغالطة، وقد تستعمل في مخاطبة العناد. ويعينها الترتيب الأنفع وحسن التصرف في استعمالها كما يعين الجدلية: فمن ذلك التطويل حتى يختلط الكلام وتتباعد أجزاء القول بعضها من بعض فیخفي توجهها إلى المطلوب. ومن ذلك الاستعمال حتى يسبق زمان العبارة زمان جودة التأمل والروية، ومن ذلك التعصب بالتعتب حتى يغلب الانفعال النفساني قوة الفكرة فيشغلها عن البينة. وجميع ذلك يعين على أن لا يحضر جميع المقدمات في الذهن؛ وإن حضرت غفل عن جهة تأديها إلى النتيجة. ومن ذلك أيضًا يغير الترتيب والوضع لإخفاء النتيجة، وكذلك خلط حجة بحجة، وقول بقول، وإبهام أنه تروّج إنتاج المطلوب. وبالجملة فالقوانين المنطقية معتبرة من طرفي السائل والمجيب فإن إظهار الحق كما يكون باعتبارها فكذلك إخفاء الحق وهو اتفاق الشبهة قصدًا. فالقياس من الجانبين قد يكون تارة صادقًا ومن صوادق، وقد يكون تارة بخلافه كما لا يكون صادقًا ولا من صوادق بل يكون بحسب الظنِّ مثلا. وأما المجيب في حل الشبهة تارة ينبغي أن يترك فيه المشهور بالحق، وتارة أن يترك الحق بالمشهور والمظنون؛ فإن الغرض في مفاوضة السوفسطائي ليس أن يقاس عليهم بالحق بل أن يُجازوا عن المراء مراء. فلا يبعد أن ينحرفوا عن الحق إلى المشهور والمظنون والقياس قد يكون باطلًا في نفسه؛ والسوفسطائي يستعمله مع علمه بأنه باطل مكانه، أو بأن يعتقد أنه صحيح في نفسه لجهله بصحة القياس. وعلى الغير أن يبين وجه البطلان بالبرهان؛ وذلك بوجوه؛ إذ القياس قد يكون باطلا بحسب مادته وقد يكون باطلا بحسب صورته، وقد يكون باطلا بحسب مادته وصورته والكل يعرف من بعد. وعلى هذا إذا كان القياس مغالطيّا فإنه قد يكون مغالطيا لمادته، وقد يكون مغالطيّا لصورته. والأول ينقض من جهة مقدماته، والثاني وإن كان يشبه في صورته القياس فإنه ليس بقياس؛ والحل قد يكون فيه من الوجهين إذا كانت المقدّمات كاذبة، فعلى الحال أن ينظر في صورته أيضًا، ويحل الشبهة منها وينظر أيضًا في النتيجة فإنه النتيجة إذا كانت كاذبة كانت متنبهة على القياس، وما فيه من الغلط. والغلط قد يكون باشتراك المفهوم على وجوهه فإنها إما أن تكون لأن السؤال يكون كثيرًا، وإما أن تكون الكثرة في النتيجة. وتلك الكثرة يكون الحق في بعضها موجودًا وفي بعضها ليس بموجود كما إذا قيل هل الساكت متكلم فإنه تغليط في النتيجة؛ فينتج نتيجتين، ولا يشعر باشتراكه وهو مقدمة بعد.
Chapter: On the use of these fallacies. These fallacies may be used for deception, and may be used in addressing the stubborn. The most beneficial order and good conduct in using them assist, just as dialectic assists. Among that: prolonging until speech becomes confused and parts of the discourse become distant from each other, hiding its direction toward the desired conclusion. And among that: haste so that the time of expression precedes the time of good reflection and deliberation. And among that: fanaticism through reproach so that emotional passion overpowers the power of thought and distracts it from the evidence. All that assists in that not all premises are present in the mind; if present, one forgets the manner of their leading to the conclusion. And among that also: changing the order and position to hide the conclusion, as well as mixing an argument with an argument, a statement with a statement, and implying that the production of the desired result is accepted. In sum, the logical laws are considered from both sides of the questioner and answerer. For making truth apparent is by considering them, as is hiding the truth—that is, intentionally causing resemblance of fallacy. So the syllogism from both sides may sometimes be true and from true premises, and sometimes the contrary, as it may be neither true nor from true premises, but according to opinion, for example. As for the answerer in resolving the fallacy, sometimes he should leave the well-known for the truth, and sometimes he should leave the truth for the well-known and the probable. For the purpose in debating the sophist is not to measure them by truth but to repay contention with contention. So it is not unlikely that they deviate from truth to the well-known and probable. A syllogism may be false in itself; the sophist uses it knowing it is false in its place, or believing it is valid in itself due to ignorance of the validity of syllogism. And it is upon another to explain the aspect of falsity by demonstration. That is in ways: for the syllogism may be false according to its matter, and may be false according to its form, and may be false according to its matter and form—all are known afterwards. And upon this, if the syllogism is fallacious, it may be fallacious in its matter, and may be fallacious in its form. The first is refuted from the side of its premises, and the second, even if it resembles a syllogism in its form, is not a syllogism. The resolution may be from both aspects if the premises are false; in that case one must also consider its form, and resolve the fallacy from it, and also consider the conclusion, for if the conclusion is false, it alerts to the syllogism and the error in it. And error may be due to equivocation of the concept in its aspects. That is because either the question is multiple, or the multiplicity is in the conclusion. That multiplicity has truth in some of them and not in others, as when it is said: 'Is the silent person speaking?' It is a deception in the conclusion; it produces two conclusions, and one is not aware of the equivocation. And it is a premise afterwards.
Source pages p. 595

Commentary
This is a chapter (فصل) on the use of fallacies (المضللات) in sophistry. The author explains that fallacies can be employed either for deliberate deception (مغالطة) or in addressing a stubborn opponent (مخاطبة العناد). Several techniques are listed: prolonging speech to confuse, speaking too quickly to avoid reflection, and using emotional reproach to distract. All these aim to prevent the opponent from keeping all premises in mind or from seeing how they lead to the conclusion. The author then turns to the logical side: the questioner and answerer must consider the logical laws (القوانين المنطقية) because truth can be hidden through them just as it can be shown. A syllogism may be true (with true premises) or false; in sophistical debate, the answerer may need to appeal to the well-known (المشهور) or probable (المظنون) rather than strict truth, to counter the sophist. Then the author classifies false syllogisms: false in matter (premises), false in form, or false in both. A fallacious syllogism (مغالطي) can be fallacious in matter or in form. The former is refuted by examining the premises; the latter, though resembling a valid form, is not actually a syllogism. In resolving equivocation (اشتراك المفهوم), error arises from multiplicity—either the question is ambiguous or the conclusion has multiple meanings. An example is given: 'Is the silent person speaking?' This equivocation produces two possible conclusions without the interlocutor realizing. This passage thus provides a comprehensive guide to identifying, using, and refuting fallacies in disputation.

Key Terms
مضللات
مغالطة
تطويل
تعصب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is one technique used to hide the direction of argument?
A) Speaking slowly and clearly
B) Prolonging speech to confuse
C) Repeating premises
D) Using formal logic

Key Takeaway
Fallacies can be used intentionally to confuse, and recognizing the techniques is the first step to refuting them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a recent public debate (e.g., on a contemporary Islamic issue) and list any techniques from this lesson that you observe being used to confuse the audience.

Sophistry and Fallacies
intermediate
5 min
Classification of False Syllogisms: Material vs Formal Fallacies
Author classifies false syllogisms into material and formal types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a syllogism false in matter from one false in form, and explain how each is refuted.

Core Idea
A false syllogism may be invalid due to false premises or invalid form; one false in form is not a real syllogism.

In this passage, the author classifies fallacious syllogisms (القياس المغالطي) into two main types: those false in their matter (premises) and those false in their form (logical structure). He explains that a syllogism that is false in form, even if it resembles a valid syllogism, is not a true syllogism at all. The refutation differs: material fallacies are refuted by examining the truth of premises, while formal fallacies are exposed by showing that the form does not guarantee the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of false syllogisms
Find the phrases 'باطلا بحسب مادته' and 'باطلا بحسب صورته' to see the classification based on matter and form.
2
Understand the refutation for material fallacies
The passage says 'ينقض من جهة مقدماته' meaning it is refuted by examining the premises—showing that one or more are false.
3
Understand the refutation for formal fallacies
The passage says 'الثاني وإن كان يشبه في صورته القياس فإنه ليس بقياس'—a formal fallacy is not a genuine syllogism; refute by showing the form is invalid.
4
Consider the combined case
The passage also mentions a syllogism false in both matter and form; resolution may require examining both aspects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : في استعمال هذه المضللات وهذه المضللات قد تستعمل للمغالطة، وقد تستعمل في مخاطبة العناد. ويعينها الترتيب الأنفع وحسن التصرف في استعمالها كما يعين الجدلية: فمن ذلك التطويل حتى يختلط الكلام وتتباعد أجزاء القول بعضها من بعض فیخفي توجهها إلى المطلوب. ومن ذلك الاستعمال حتى يسبق زمان العبارة زمان جودة التأمل والروية، ومن ذلك التعصب بالتعتب حتى يغلب الانفعال النفساني قوة الفكرة فيشغلها عن البينة. وجميع ذلك يعين على أن لا يحضر جميع المقدمات في الذهن؛ وإن حضرت غفل عن جهة تأديها إلى النتيجة. ومن ذلك أيضًا يغير الترتيب والوضع لإخفاء النتيجة، وكذلك خلط حجة بحجة، وقول بقول، وإبهام أنه تروّج إنتاج According to the passage, how is a syllogism that is false in form refuted?
A) By pointing out that its premises are false

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Sophistry and Fallacies

Equivocation: The Fallacy of Multiple Meanings
Author explains equivocation as a source of fallacy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify equivocation in a question or conclusion and explain how it leads to deception.

Core Idea
Equivocation hides the fact that a term or question has multiple meanings, causing an argument to shift.

The author identifies equivocation (اشتراك المفهوم) as a specific source of error in fallacious reasoning. Error arises because either the question has multiple meanings, or the conclusion can be interpreted in multiple ways. An example is given: 'Is the silent person speaking?' This question can be answered yes if 'speaking' includes inner speech, or no if it means audible speech. The fallacy lies in producing two conclusions without the interlocutor noticing.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of equivocation
Find the phrase 'الغلط قد يكون باشتراك المفهوم' to see the starting point of the discussion on equivocation.
2
Understand the two sources of multiplicity
The passage says 'إما أن تكون لأن السؤال يكون كثيرًا، وإما أن تكون الكثرة في النتيجة'—multiplicity can be in the question or in the conclusion.
3
Analyze the example
The example 'هل الساكت متكلم' is given. Determine that it can yield two different answers depending on the meaning of 'speaking'.
4
Explain why it is deceptive
The equivocation produces two conclusions without the hearer realizing the semantic shift, thus creating a false impression of a valid argument.

And upon this, if the syllogism is fallacious, it may be fallacious in its matter, and may be fallacious in its form. The first is refuted from the side of its premises, and the second, even if it resembles a syllogism in its form, is not a syllogism. The resolution may be from both aspects if the premises are false; in that case one must also consider its form, and resolve the fallacy from it, and also consider the conclusion, for if the conclusion is false, it alerts to the syllogism and the error in it. And error may be due to equivocation of the concept in its aspects. That is because either the question is multiple, or the multiplicity is in the conclusion. That multiplicity has truth in some of them and not in others, as when it is said: 'Is the silent person speaking?' It is a deception in the conclusion; it produces two conclusions, and one is not aware of the equivocation. And it is a premise afterwards.
Source pages p. 595

Commentary
This is a chapter (فصل) on the use of fallacies (المضللات) in sophistry. The author explains that fallacies can be employed either for deliberate deception (مغالطة) or in addressing a stubborn opponent (مخاطبة العناد). Several techniques are listed: prolonging speech to confuse, speaking too quickly to avoid reflection, and using emotional reproach to distract. All these aim to prevent the opponent from keeping all premises in mind or from seeing how they lead to the conclusion. The author then turns to the logical side: the questioner and answerer must consider the logical laws (القوانين المنطقية) because truth can be hidden through them just as it can be shown. A syllogism may be true (with true premises) or false; in sophistical debate, the answerer may need to appeal to the well-known (المشهور) or probable (المظنون) rather than strict truth, to counter the sophist. Then the author classifies false syllogisms: false in matter (premises), false in form, or false in both. A fallacious syllogism (مغالطي) can be fallacious in matter or in form. The former is refuted by examining the premises; the latter, though resembling a valid form, is not actually a syllogism. In resolving equivocation (اشتراك المفهوم), error arises from multiplicity—either the question is ambiguous or the conclusion has multiple meanings. An example is given: 'Is the silent person speaking?' This equivocation produces two possible conclusions without the interlocutor realizing. This passage thus provides a comprehensive guide to identifying, using, and refuting fallacies in disputation.

Key Terms
اشتراك المفهوم
الساكت
متكلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the example 'Is the silent person speaking?' illustrate according to the passage?
A) A syllogism false in form
B) Equivocation causing multiplicity in conclusion
C) A material fallacy
D) The use of emotional reproach

Key Takeaway
Equivocation exploits multiple meanings to produce deceptive conclusions; awareness of this fallacy sharpens critical reasoning.

Sophistry and Fallacies

Verbal Fallacies: Neglect of Wording and Conventional Meaning
Author on resolving verbal fallacies through wording analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify verbal fallacies caused by neglecting absolute vs. restricted wording or the first vs. second conventional meaning, and explain how to resolve them.

Core Idea
Neglecting the wording's absolute/restricted state or its conventional layer breeds fallacy.

Many fallacies in Islamic logic arise from carelessness with language. The author teaches us that when a fallacy stems from ignoring how a word is used—whether absolute or restricted, or in its first or second conventional meaning—the solution lies in precise linguistic analysis. This lesson will equip you to spot and correct such verbal fallacies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the word causing confusion
Locate the lafẓ that is disputed or ambiguous in the argument. This is the term whose meaning is at the heart of the fallacy.
2
Check for absolute vs. restricted usage
Determine whether the word is used in an unqualified sense (muṭlaq) or with a qualification (muqayyad). Mistaking one for the other is a common source of error.
3
Determine the conventional meaning (waḍʿ)
Assess whether the word is used in its original coinage (al-waḍʿ al-awwal) or a secondary technical meaning (al-waḍʿ al-thānī). Confusing these leads to invalid inferences.
4
Apply the correct interpretation
Based on the above analysis, clarify what the word actually indicates and evaluate the argument accordingly.
The resolution always involves specifying the word's state and conventional layer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة إذا كانت المغالطة بالإغفال عن رعاية اللفظ وإطلاقه وتقييده ودلالته على الشيء بوضعه أو بغير وضعه، وما يكون بالوضع فذلك بالوضع أولا أو ثانيا؛ فبيان ذلك الغلط لا بد وأن يكون برعاية اللفظ وتقريره بأنه إذا كان مطلقا كذا، وإذا كان مقيدًا كذا. وكذلك في الوضع الأول كذا، وفي الوضع الثاني كذا. وعلى هذا إذا كانت المغالطة بالإغفال عن رعاية المعنى؛ والمعنى قد يكون واضحًا مستمرًا في البعض من الصور، وقد لا يكون كذلك بل يكون متشبها فإنه يكون معينا في نفسه وإن لم يكن معينًا في علمنا، أو لا يكون معينًا بل يكون أمرًا مشتركا بين الشيء وغيره؛ فينظر فيما يكون سببًا للغلط ويبين بما يبين به ذلك إذا الكلام وإن كان حقا؛ فإذا لم يكن مناسبا للغرض فلا يكون مفيدا يحصل منه الغرض حتى إذا كان الغرض أن يكون الإنسان ضحاكًا. فقولنا كل إنسان حيوان، وكل حيوان حساس فإنه لا يكون مفيدا مع كونه حقا. ولا يظن أنه إذا لم يكن مفيدا يحصل منه الغرض لا يكون حقا؛ إذ كونه حقا من جهة أنه صادق لا مجال للشبهة فيه . وكونه غير مفيد من جهة أنه لا يكون مناسبًا للغرض؛ فحقيقة الشيء بالنسبة إليه في نفس الأمر لا بالنسبة إلى ما هو الغرض. نعم يقال للشيء إنه في كونه مفيدا لذلك حق لكنه إنما يكون حقا في كونه مفيدا لذلك لما أنه مفيد لذلك في نفس الأمر حتى إذا لم يكن كذلك لم يكن حقا. وإذا لم يكن حقا على الإطلاق لا يلزم أن يكون باطلا فإنه يمكن أن يكون الشيء حقا من وجه، وباطلاً من وجه كما في قولنا إن هذا الإنسان إنسان فإنه حق من حيث إنه لا يكون غير إنسان، وباطل من حيث إنه يكون خاليا عن الفائدة. وقد كان هذا من جملة إيضاح الواضحات لا من حيث إنه مشتمل على التكرار ؛ والتكرار لا يكون باطلاً في جميع الصور بل يكون لازما في البعض من الصور كما مر في تعريف الإضافيات وغيرها، فاعتبر بما عرفت وبالله التوفيق.
In summary, if the sophism is due to neglecting the consideration of the wording, its absoluteness, its restriction, its indication to the thing by its conventional meaning or by other than its conventional meaning, and what is by convention, that is by first or second conventional meaning; then the clarification of that error must be by consideration of the wording and its determination: that if it is absolute it is such, and if restricted it is such. Likewise, in the first conventional meaning it is such, and in the second conventional meaning it is such. And upon this, if the sophism is due to neglecting the consideration of the meaning; and the meaning may be clear and consistent in some forms, and may not be so but rather ambiguous, then it is determinate in itself even if not determinate in our knowledge, or it is not determinate but is a common thing between the thing and other than it. Then one looks into what is the cause of error and clarifies it by what clarifies it. For a statement, even if it is true, if it is not suitable for the purpose, it is not useful to achieve the purpose. For example, if the purpose is that man is a laughing animal, then our saying 'Every man is an animal, and every animal is sentient' is not useful despite being true. And one should not think that if it is not useful to achieve the purpose, it is not true; for its being true is from the aspect that it is truthful, with no room for doubt in it. And its being not useful is from the aspect that it is not suitable for the purpose. So the reality of the thing is relative to it in itself, not relative to what the purpose is. Yes, one says of a thing that it is true in its being useful for that, but it is only true in its being useful for that because it is useful for that in itself, such that if it were not so, it would not be true. And if it is not true absolutely, it does not necessarily follow that it is false, for a thing can be true from one aspect and false from another, as in our saying 'This man is a man'; it is true in that he is not not a man, and false in that it is devoid of benefit. And this is from the clarification of the obvious, not from the aspect that it involves repetition; repetition is not false in all forms but is necessary in some forms, as has passed in the definition of relationals and others. So consider what you have known, and with God is success.
Source pages p. 597

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of fallacies arising from neglect of linguistic precision and the distinction between truth and usefulness. The author first addresses verbal fallacies (al-mughālata bil-ighfāl 'an ri'āyat al-lafẓ). When a fallacy arises from ignoring the absolute or restricted nature of a word, or its first or second conventional meaning (al-waḍ'), the solution is to clarify the wording accordingly. The same applies to fallacies of meaning (al-ma'nā): meaning may be clear or ambiguous, determinate in itself or common. The resolver must identify the cause of error. The author then makes an important epistemological point: a statement can be true yet not useful if it does not serve the purpose of the argument. The example given: if the goal is to prove that man is a laughing animal, the syllogism 'Every man is an animal, every animal is sentient' is true but useless because it does not lead to the intended conclusion. Being true (ḥaqq) is about correspondence to reality (ṣādiq), while being useful (mufīd) is about appropriateness to the goal. The author warns against confusing these. A statement can be true from one aspect and false from another, as in the tautology 'This man is a man'—true in essence but false in terms of benefit. Repetition (takrār) is not always false; sometimes it is necessary, as in definitions of relational concepts. The passage ends with a call to reflect.

Key Terms
اللفظ
الوضع
الإطلاق
التقييد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what two aspects of a word must be considered to resolve a verbal fallacy?
A) Its etymology and usage
B) Its absoluteness/restriction and conventional meaning
C) Its literal and metaphorical meaning
D) Its homonymy and synonymy

Key Takeaway
Verbal fallacies are resolved by analyzing the word's absolute/restricted usage and its conventional layer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Fallacies of Meaning: Clear, Ambiguous, and Common
Author on classifying fallacies of meaning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify fallacies of meaning into those where the meaning is clear, ambiguous but determinate in itself, or common (shared) between multiple things, and explain how to correct each type.

Core Idea
Fallacies of meaning arise when we mishandle ambiguity—whether the meaning is clear, determinate but unknown, or shared.

Just as wording can cause fallacies, so can meaning. The author categorizes the states of meaning: sometimes it is clear and consistent, sometimes ambiguous. When ambiguous, it may be determinate in itself but unknown to us, or it may be common (mushtarak) across multiple things. Recognizing which type we face is key to resolving the fallacy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term whose meaning is contested
Locate the word whose meaning is being debated and causing the logical error.
2
Check if the meaning is clear and consistent
If the meaning is clear and consistent in all contexts (wāḍiḥ mustamirr), there is no fallacy from this angle.
3
Assess the type of ambiguity
If the meaning is ambiguous (mutashābih), determine whether it is determinate in itself (muʿayyan fī nafsih) even if unknown to us, or if it is common (mushtarak) between multiple meanings.
Determinate but unknown: the word has one real meaning, but we are unsure what it is. Common: the word has more than one meaning.
4
Clarify based on the type
Look into the cause of error specific to that type and clarify accordingly. For determinate but unknown, investigation may reveal the actual meaning. For common, the intended meaning must be specified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Truth vs. Utility in Arguments
Author distinguishing truth from usefulness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a true argument (ḥaqq as ṣādiq) and a useful argument (mufīd), and identify when a true argument is not useful.

Core Idea
Truth is about correspondence; usefulness is about relevance. A true argument can be useless if it misses the purpose.

A statement can be true yet completely unhelpful for the goal at hand. The author illustrates this with the syllogism 'Every man is an animal, every animal is sentient' when the purpose is to prove that man is a laughing animal. This lesson will help you separate truth from utility, a crucial distinction in Islamic debate and reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of the argument (gharaḍ)
Determine what the argument is supposed to prove or achieve. In the author's example, the purpose is to show that man is a laughing animal.
2
Check if the premises are true
Verify that each premise corresponds to reality. In the example, 'Every man is an animal' and 'Every animal is sentient' are both true.
3
Assess relevance to the purpose
Even if true, do the premises lead to the desired conclusion? The example's premises conclude that man is sentient, not that man is a laughing animal. They are irrelevant to the purpose.
4
Conclude on truth and utility separately
The argument is true (ṣādiq) but not useful (mufīd) for the purpose. One should not think that lack of utility implies falsehood; truth and utility are independent dimensions.
A statement can be true from one aspect and false from another, as in tautologies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة إذا كانت المغالطة بالإغفال عن رعاية اللفظ وإطلاقه وتقييده ودلالته على الشيء بوضعه أو بغير وضعه، وما يكون بالوضع فذلك بالوضع أولا أو ثانيا؛ فبيان ذلك الغلط لا بد وأن يكون برعاية اللفظ وتقريره بأنه إذا كان مطلقا كذا، وإذا كان مقيدًا كذا. وكذلك في الوضع الأول كذا، وفي الوضع الثاني كذا. وعلى هذا إذا كانت المغالطة بالإغفال عن رعاية المعنى؛ والمعنى قد يكون واضحًا مستمرًا في البعض من الصور، وقد لا يكون كذلك بل يكون متشبها فإنه يكون معينا في نفسه وإن لم يكن معينًا في علمنا، أو لا يكون معينًا بل يكون أمرًا مشتركا بين الشيء وغيره؛ فينظر فيما يكون سببًا للغلط ويبين بما يبين به ذلك إذا الكلام وإن كان حقا؛ فإذا لم يكن مناسبا للغرض فلا يكون مفيدا يحصل منه الغرض حتى إذا كان الغرض أن يكون الإنسان ضحاكًا. فقولنا كل إنسان حيوان، وكل حيوان حساس فإنه لا يكون مفيدا مع كونه حقا. ولا يظن أنه إذا لم يكن مفيدا يحصل منه الغرض لا يكون حقا؛ إذ كونه حقا من جهة أنه صادق لا مجال للشبهة فيه . وكونه غير مفيد من جهة أنه لا يكون مناسبًا للغرض؛ فحقيقة الشيء بالنسبة إليه في نفس الأمر لا بالنسبة إلى ما هو الغرض. نعم يقال للشيء إنه في كونه مفيدا لذلك حق لكنه إنما يكون حقا في كونه مفيدا لذلك لما أنه مفيد لذلك في نفس الأمر حتى إذا لم يكن كذلك لم يكن حقا. وإذا لم يكن حقا على الإطلاق لا يلزم أن يكون باطلا فإنه يمكن أن يكون الشيء حقا من وجه، وباطلاً من وجه كما في قولنا إن هذا الإنسان إنسان فإنه حق من حيث إنه لا يكون غير إنسان، وباطل من حيث إنه يكون خاليا عن الفائدة. وقد كان هذا من جملة إيضاح الواضحات لا من حيث إنه مشتمل على التكرار ؛ والتكرار لا يكون باطلاً في جميع الصور بل يكون لازما في البعض من الصور كما مر في تعريف الإضافيات وغيرها، فاعتبر بما عرفت وبالله التوفيق.
In summary, if the sophism is due to neglecting the consideration of the wording, its absoluteness, its restriction, its indication to the thing by its conventional meaning or by other than its conventional meaning, and what is by convention, that is by first or second conventional meaning; then the clarification of that error must be by consideration of the wording and its determination: that if it is absolute it is such, and if restricted it is such. Likewise, in the first conventional meaning it is such, and in the second conventional meaning it is such. And upon this, if the sophism is due to neglecting the consideration of the meaning; and the meaning may be clear and consistent in some forms, and may not be so but rather ambiguous, then it is determinate in itself even if not determinate in our knowledge, or it is not determinate but is a common thing between the thing and other than it. For a statement, even if it is true, if it is not suitable for the purpose, it is not useful to achieve the purpose. For example, if the purpose is that man is a laughing animal, then our saying 'Every man is an animal, and every animal is sentient' is not useful despite being true. And one should not think that if it is not useful to achieve the purpose, it is not true; for its being true is from the aspect that it is truthful, with no room for doubt in it. And its being not useful is from the aspect that it is not suitable for the purpose. So the reality of the thing is relative to it in itself, not relative to what the purpose is. Yes, one says of a thing that it is true in its being useful for that, but it is only true in its being useful for that because it is useful for that in itself, such that if it were not so, it would not be true. And if it is not true absolutely, it does not necessarily follow that it is false, for a thing can be true from one aspect and false from another, as in our saying 'This man is a man'; it is true in that he is not not a man, and false in that it is devoid of benefit. And this is from the clarification of the obvious, not from the aspect that it involves repetition; repetition is not false in all forms but is necessary in some forms, as has passed in the definition of relationals and others. So consider what you have known, and with God is success.
Source pages p. 597

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of fallacies arising from neglect of linguistic precision and the distinction between truth and usefulness. The author first addresses verbal fallacies (al-mughālata bil-ighfāl 'an ri'āyat al-lafẓ). When a fallacy arises from ignoring the absolute or restricted nature of a word, or its first or second conventional meaning (al-waḍ'), the solution is to clarify the wording accordingly. The same applies to fallacies of meaning (al-ma'nā): meaning may be clear or ambiguous, determinate in itself or common. The resolver must identify the cause of error. The author then makes an important epistemological point: a statement can be true yet not useful if it does not serve the purpose of the argument. The example given: if the goal is to prove that man is a laughing animal, the syllogism 'Every man is an animal, every animal is sentient' is true but useless because it does not lead to the intended conclusion. Being true (ḥaqq) is about correspondence to reality (ṣādiq), while being useful (mufīd) is about appropriateness to the goal. The author warns against confusing these. A statement can be true from one aspect and false from another, as in the tautology 'This man is a man'—true in essence but false in terms of benefit. Repetition (takrār) is not always false; sometimes it is necessary, as in definitions of relational concepts. The passage ends with a call to reflect.

Key Terms
الحق
المفيد
الغرض
الصادق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the syllogism 'Every man is an animal, every animal is sentient' not useful when the purpose is to prove that man is a laughing animal?
A) Because it contains a false premise.
B) Because it is a tautology.
C) Because it is not suitable for the purpose, even though it is true.
D) Because it is a sophistical fallacy.

Key Takeaway
An argument can be true yet not useful; truth is about correspondence, utility about relevance to the purpose.

Tautology and Repetition: When Is It Acceptable?
Author on the acceptability of repetition in definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a tautology is fallacious and when it is necessary, especially in definitions of relational terms.

Core Idea
Repetition is not always false; in definitions of relationals, it is logically necessary.

The statement 'This man is a man' seems empty, yet it is true. The author points out that repetition (takrār) is not automatically false; sometimes it is unavoidable, particularly in defining relational terms (al-iḍāfiyyāt). This lesson will help you judge when repetition is a real fallacy and when it is a legitimate part of definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the tautological statement
Look for a statement that repeats the same term or idea, such as 'This man is a man'.
2
Check if the statement is informative
Does it add new information beyond what is already understood? If it is purely repetitive with no new content, it is tautological.
3
Determine if the statement is part of a definition of a relational term
In definitions of relationals (iḍāfī), like 'father' or 'brother', the definition often involves the very relation being defined, leading to unavoidable repetition. The author notes this is sometimes necessary.
4
Decide whether the repetition is fallacious or necessary
If the repetition is empty and not part of a necessary definition, it is a fallacy (false in terms of benefit). If it is a legitimate part of defining a relational, it is not false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة إذا كانت المغالطة بالإغفال عن رعاية اللفظ وإطلاقه وتقييده ودلالته على الشيء بوضعه أو بغير وضعه، وما يكون بالوضع فذلك بالوضع أولا أو ثانيا؛ فبيان ذلك الغلط لا بد وأن يكون برعاية اللفظ وتقريره بأنه إذا كان مطلقا كذا، وإذا كان مقيدًا كذا. وكذلك في الوضع الأول كذا، وفي الوضع الثاني كذا. وعلى هذا إذا كانت المغالطة بالإغفال عن رعاية المعنى؛ والمعنى قد يكون واضحًا مستمرًا في البعض من الصور، وقد لا يكون كذلك بل يكون متشبها فإنه يكون معينا في نفسه وإن لم يكن معينًا في علمنا، أو لا يكون معينًا بل يكون أمرًا مشتركا بين الشيء وغيره؛ فينظر فيما يكون سببًا للغلط ويبين بما يبين به ذلك إذا الكلام وإن كان حقا؛ فإذا لم يكن مناسبا للغرض فلا يكون مفيدا يحصل منه الغرض حتى إذا كان الغرض أن يكون الإنسان ضحاكًا. فقولنا كل إنسان حيوان، وكل حيوان حساس فإنه لا يكون مفيدا مع كونه حقا. ولا يظن أنه إذا لم يكن مفيدا يحصل منه الغرض لا يكون حقا؛ إذ كونه حقا من جهة أنه صادق لا مجال للشبهة فيه . وكونه غير مفيد من جهة أنه لا يكون مناسبًا للغرض؛ فحقيقة الشيء بالنسبة إليه في نفس الأمر لا بالنسبة إلى ما هو الغرض. نعم يقال للشيء إنه في كونه مفيدا لذلك حق لكنه إنما يكون حقا في كونه مفيدا لذلك لما أنه مفيد لذلك في نفس الأمر حتى إذا لم يكن كذلك لم يكن حقا. وإذا لم يكن حقا على الإطلاق لا يلزم أن يكون باطلا فإنه يمكن أن يكون الشيء حقا من وجه، وباطلاً من وجه كما في قولنا إن هذا الإنسان إنسان فإنه حق من حيث إنه لا يكون غير إنسان، وباطل من حيث إنه يكون خاليا عن الفائدة. وقد كان هذا من جملة إيضاح الواضحات لا من حيث إنه مشتمل على التكرار ؛ والتكرار لا يكون باطلاً في جميع الصور بل يكون لازما في البعض من الصور كما مر في تعريف الإضافيات وغيرها، فاعتبر بما عرفت وبالله التوفيق.
In summary, if the sophism is due to neglecting the consideration of the wording, its absoluteness, its restriction, its indication to the thing by its conventional meaning or by other than its conventional meaning, and what is by convention, that is by first or second conventional meaning; then the clarification of that error must be by consideration of the wording and its determination: that if it is absolute it is such, and if restricted it is such. Likewise, in the first conventional meaning it is such, and in the second conventional meaning it is such. Fallacies from Neglect of Wording and Meaning
Author discusses fallacies from neglect of wording or meaning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish truth from benefit in evaluating statements and identify fallacies arising from neglecting wording or meaning.

Core Idea
A statement can be true but useless if it misses the purpose.

This passage explores two sources of fallacy: overlooking the precise wording of a statement and ignoring the intended meaning. The author emphasizes that even a true statement can be fallacious if it fails to serve the intended purpose. Understanding this distinction helps you avoid errors in reasoning, especially when applying logical analysis to Islamic sciences such as usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sources of fallacy
Note that the author classifies fallacies into those arising from neglecting wording and those from neglecting meaning. For wording, consider whether it is absolute or restricted, and whether it signifies by primary or secondary coinage. For meaning, consider whether it is clear or ambiguous.
This classification is foundational for analyzing statements.
2
Distinguish truth from benefit
The author stresses that a statement can be true (haqiqa) without being beneficial (fa'ida) if it does not suit the purpose. For example, 'Every man is an animal, and every animal is sentient' is true but useless if the goal is to define man as a laughing being.
Truth is correspondence to reality; benefit is relevance to the goal.
3
Apply the distinction to an Islamic example
Consider the Qur'anic verse 'Kill the polytheists' (9:5). The statement is true as a command, but its benefit depends on context: it is restricted to combatants in a specific state of war. Neglecting the restriction leads to a fallacy of meaning.
This illustrates how ignoring wording or meaning can cause error in Islamic legal reasoning.
4
Recognize when repetition is necessary
The author notes that repetition is not always false; it may be required in definitions, such as relational terms. For instance, defining 'father' as 'parent of a child' uses repetition but is necessary for clarity.
This refines the understanding of what constitutes a fallacy.

And in summary, if the fallacy is by neglecting the observance of the wording, its absoluteness and its restriction, and its indication of the thing by its coinage or other than its coinage, and what is by coinage, that is by coinage first or second; then the explanation of that error must be by observing the wording and determining it: that if it is absolute, it is such; and if it is restricted, it is such. Similarly, in the first coinage it is such, and in the second coinage it is such. And upon this, if the fallacy is by neglecting the observance of the meaning; and the meaning may be clear and continuous in some forms, and may not be so but rather ambiguous, then it is determined in itself even if not determined in our knowledge, or it is not determined but is something common between the thing and other than it; then one looks into what causes the error and explains it by what explains that, because speech, even if true, if it is not suitable for the purpose, then it is not beneficial from which the purpose is obtained; even if the purpose is that man is a laugher. So our statement 'Every man is an animal, and every animal is sentient' is not beneficial despite being true. And one should not think that if it is not beneficial to obtain the purpose, it is not true; since its being true is with respect to its being truthful, with no room for doubt in it. And its being non-beneficial is with respect to its not being suitable for the purpose; so the truth of the thing is relative to it in itself, not relative to what is the purpose. Yes, it is said of something that in its being beneficial for that purpose it is true, but it is only true in its being beneficial for that purpose because it is beneficial for that purpose in itself, such that if it were not so, it would not be true. And this [348] was part of clarifying the obvious, not because it involves repetition; and repetition is not false in all forms but is necessary in some forms, as passed in the definition of relations and others, so take heed of what you have learned, and with Allah is success.

Commentary
This passage discusses fallacies that arise from neglecting either the wording (lafz) or the meaning (ma'na) of statements. The author explains that to expose such errors, one must attend to whether the wording is absolute or restricted, and whether it signifies by primary coinage (wad') or secondary coinage. Similarly, for meaning, it may be clear or ambiguous. A key point is that a statement can be true but not beneficial if it does not serve the purpose at hand. For example, saying 'Every man is an animal, and every animal is sentient' is true but useless if the goal is to define man as a laughing animal. Truth is a matter of correspondence to reality, while benefit is a matter of relevance to the goal. Thus, a statement can be true in one respect and false in another, like 'This man is a man' – true as an identity, but false as a statement lacking benefit. The passage also notes that repetition is not always false; sometimes it is necessary, as in definitions of relational terms. The author concludes by urging the reader to consider these points.

The Fallacy of Homonymy in Logical Arguments
Analyzing the fallacy of homonymy with the 'possible' argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy of homonymy (ishtirāk al-lafẓ) in a theological argument and explain how it undermines the conclusion.

Core Idea
Homonymy hides equivocation: one word, two meanings, and an invalid conclusion.

The first verbal fallacy the author discusses is homonymy, where a single word carries multiple meanings. The example involving 'possible' (mumkin) applied to the Necessary Being shows how confusion between the general and specific senses leads to an apparent contradiction. This lesson dissects that example to train you to spot such equivocations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous term
In the argument, find the word 'mumkin' (possible). Notice it is used in two different senses: one meaning 'contingent' (possible of existence and non-existence) and another meaning 'non-impossible' (which includes the Necessary Being).
The argument only works if the same sense is used throughout.
2
Trace the argument's structure
The argument claims: (1) The Necessary Being is either possible of existence or not. (2) If possible, then everything possible can cease to exist, so the Necessary Being could cease – impossible. (3) If not possible, then it is impossible. The dilemma seems to show the Necessary Being is impossible.
This is a form of reductio ad absurdum.
3
Locate the shift in meaning
The first premise uses 'possible' in the broad sense (non-impossible). The second premise uses 'possible' in the narrow sense (contingent). The conclusion conflates them.
The Necessary Being is possible in the broad sense but not in the narrow sense.
4
Formulate the correction
When the term is disambiguated, the argument fails: the Necessary Being is possible in the broad sense (it is not impossible), but not possible in the narrow sense (it cannot not exist). No contradiction arises.
This illustrates why homonymy is a material fallacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ب] وأما الذي من جهة المادة وهي المقدمات فذلك من جملة ما قد مرَّ الكلام فيه من الاشتراك في اللفظ وغيره؛ فإن الغلط لا يكون واقعا إلا بسبب الاشتباه في اللفظ أو بسبب الاشتباه في المعنى. أما في اللفظ [١] فمنه ما ينشأ من نفس اللفظ كقول القائل إن الواجب إذا كان موجودًا فإما أن يكون ممكن الوجود، أو لا يكون؛ فإن كان ممكن الوجود وكل ما يمكن وجوده يمكن عدمه فالواجب مما يمكن عدمه وذلك محال. وإن لم يكن ممكن الوجود وكل ما لا يمكن وجوده فهو ممتنع؛ فالواجب ممتنع، وإنه محال أيضًا. ووجه الغلط فيه باشتراك اللفظ وهو الممكن بين الممكن الخاص والعام والعام هو اللا ممتنع والممكن بهذا المعنى لا يجب أن يكون ممكن العدم؛ إذ الواجب لذاته ممكن بهذا المعنى ؛ وبغير هذا المعنى لا يمكن أن يوصف به الواجب. [٢] ومنه ما ينشأ من صيغته نحو القابل فإن صيغته الفاعل فيظن أن القبول فعل حتى يقال إن الهيولى فاعلة على أنها قابلة؛ [٣] ومنه ما ينشأ من التصريف كلفظ المختار فإنه يمكن أن يكون فاعلا ويمكن أن يكون مفعولاً. وكذلك في قولنا ضرب زيد فإنه يمكن أن يكون زيد فاعلا ويمكن أن يكون مفعولاً لما أن المصدر يضاف تارة إلى الفاعل وتارة إلى المفعول؛ [٤] ومنه ما ينشأ من الإعجام والتشديد والتخفيف وغير ذلك على ما عرف؛ [٥] ومنه [٣٤٩] ما يكون من كون اللفظ / مجازا كما يقال إنه تعالى نور، والنور محسوس فالله تعالى محسوس؛ ولا يلزم أن يكون كذلك. وإطلاق لفظ النور على الله سبحانه بطريق المجاز. وأما في المعنى فمثل ما مرّ [٦] من سوء اعتبار الحمل [۷] والمصادرة على المطلوب الأول، [۸] وجعل ما ليس بعلة علة، [۹] وغير ذلك من تركيب المفصل، [١٠] وتفصيل المركب [١١] وأخذ اللازم مكان الملزوم ، [۱۲] وأخذ ما بالعرض مكان ما بالذات، [١٣ ] وأخذ ما بالقوة مكان ما بالفعل [١٤] وبالعكس في الأمثلة المذكورة. وما عدا تلك الأمثلة فلها من الأمثلة من كل نوع ؛ وذلك يعرف بالتأمل. [ت] ولما ظهر عليك ما يكون من أسباب الغلط الواقع في الصورة تارة وفي المادة أخرى فقد ظهر أيضًا ما يكون من كل واحدة منهما كما إذا كان القياس منحرفًا عن الصورة القياسية وهى التأليف، وعن مادتها وهي المقدمات الستة
[B] As for that which is from the aspect of matter, i.e., the premises, that is among what has already been discussed concerning verbal homonymy and other things. For error only occurs due to confusion in wording or confusion in meaning. As for wording: [1] from it is what arises from the word itself, like the claim: 'If the Necessary Being exists, then either it is possible of existence or not. If it is possible of existence, and everything possible of existence is possible of non-existence, then the Necessary Being is among that which is possible of non-existence, and that is impossible. And if it is not possible of existence, and everything not possible of existence is impossible, then the Necessary Being is impossible, and that is also impossible.' The aspect of error in it is due to the homonymy of the word 'possible' between the specific possible and the general (the general is the non-impossible). 'Possible' in this sense does not require that it be possible of non-existence; indeed, the Necessary Being in itself is possible in this sense, and in no other sense can it be described as possible. [2] From it is what arises from its form, like the word 'al-qabil' (the receiver): its form is fa'il (active participle), so one thinks that receiving is an action, such that it is said that hyle is acting while it is receptive. [3] From it is what arises from conjugation, like the word 'al-mukhtar' — it can be an active agent or a passive object. Likewise in our saying 'Zayd was struck' (daraba Zayd), Zayd can be the subject (striker) or the object (struck) because the verbal noun is sometimes attributed to the subject and sometimes to the object. [4] From it is what arises from diacritical marks, gemination, lightening, and other things as is known. [5] From it is what arises from the word being metaphorical, as when it is said that He (exalted) is light, and light is perceptible, so God (exalted) is perceptible; but that does not necessarily follow. The application of the word 'light' to God (glorified) is by way of metaphor. As for meaning, it is like what has passed: [6] ill-considered predication, [7] begging the question (petitio principii), [8] taking what is not a cause as a cause, [9] and other things like composition of the divided, [10] division of the composed, [11] taking the necessary along with the necessary-less, [12] taking the accidental for the essential, [13] taking the potential for the actual, [14] and the reverse in the mentioned examples. Apart from those examples, there are examples for each type; that is known by reflection. [T] When it has become clear to you what are the causes of error occurring sometimes in form and other times in matter, it has also become clear what results from both together, as when the syllogism deviates from the syllogistic form, which is composition, and from its matter, which are the six premises.
Source pages p. 599

Commentary
This passage continues the comprehensive summary of fallacies, turning to material errors. Material fallacies are divided into two broad categories: those caused by confusion in words (al-ashbāh fī al-lafẓ) and those caused by confusion in meaning (al-ashbāh fī al-maʿnā). The author illustrates verbal fallacies with several examples. The first is the fallacy of homonymy (ishtirāk), using the word 'possible' (mumkin) as applied to the Necessary Being (al-wājib). The fallacy arises because 'possible' can mean either 'contingent' (possible of existence and non-existence) or 'non-impossible' (which includes the Necessary Being). The argument incorrectly concludes that the Necessary Being is impossible by confusing the two senses. Secondly, the fallacy of form (ṣīgha) is shown with 'al-qābil' (receiver): its active participle form suggests that receiving is an action, whereas in reality hyle (matter) is passive. Thirdly, the fallacy of conjugation (taṣrīf) is illustrated with 'al-mukhtār' which can be active or passive, and the ambiguous phrase 'ḍaraba Zayd' can mean either 'Zayd struck' or 'Zayd was struck'. Fourthly, errors from diacritics (iʿjām), gemination (tashdīd), and lightening (takhfīf) are mentioned. Fifthly, the fallacy of metaphor (majāz) is seen when 'light' is applied to God; one might mistakenly infer that because light is sensible, God is sensible, but the term is used metaphorically. The passage then turns to conceptual fallacies, listing them by number: [6] ill-considered predication (sūʾ iʿtibār al-ḥaml), [7] begging the question (al-muṣādara ʿalā al-maṭlūb al-awwal), [8] false cause (jaʿl mā laysa bi-ʿilla ʿilla), [9] composition of the divided (tarkīb al-mufaṣṣal), [10] division of the composed (tafṣīl al-murakkab), [11] taking the necessary along with the necessary-less (akhdh al-lāzim makān al-malzūm), [12] taking the accidental for the essential (akhdh mā bi-al-ʿaraḍ makān mā bi-al-dhāt), [13] taking the potential for the actual (akhdh mā bi-al-quwwa makān mā bi-al-fiʿl), and [14] the reverse. The author notes that there are examples for each type and that they can be known through reflection. Finally, a concluding remark [T] states that when errors of form and matter are understood, errors combining both are also evident, such as a syllogism that deviates both in its structure (composition) and in its matter (the six kinds of premises).

Key Terms
اشتراك اللفظ
ممكن
واجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the argument about the Necessary Being, what is the two meanings of 'possible' that are conflated?
A) Essential and accidental
B) General (non-impossible) and specific (contingent)
C) Actual and potential
D) Necessary and contingent

Key Takeaway
Homonymy causes fallacies when a single word carries two distinct meanings that are used interchangeably in an argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the argument: 'Wine is prohibited because it causes intoxication. Nabidh also causes intoxication, so nabidh is prohibited.' Does this contain a fallacy of homonymy? Identify the ambiguous term and explain why or why not.
?
Find another example in Islamic legal reasoning where a word like 'riba' (usury) might be used equivocally. How would you resolve it?

Sophistry and Fallacies
intermediate
6 min
The Fallacy of Metaphor in Theological Discourse
Analyzing the fallacy of metaphor with the 'light' example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to detect when a metaphorical expression is mistakenly treated as literal in a theological argument, causing a fallacy.

Core Idea
Metaphor is not literal; treating it as such leads to false conclusions about God.

The fifth verbal fallacy the author mentions is majāz (metaphorical usage). The example of calling God 'light' (nūr) shows how a metaphorical statement can be exploited to draw an absurd literal conclusion. This lesson trains you to identify such fallacies in religious texts and debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the metaphorical term
Find the word 'nūr' (light) applied to God. In the Qur'an, God is described as 'light of the heavens and the earth' (24:35), but this is understood metaphorically.
Metaphor is common in describing God's attributes.
2
Extract the argument
The argument goes: God is light (premise 1). Light is perceptible by the senses (premise 2). Therefore, God is perceptible (conclusion).
This is a categorical syllogism in form, but the first premise is metaphorical.
3
Spot the fallacy
The fallacy is assuming that 'light' is used literally in the first premise. Since it is metaphorical, the term 'light' in premise 1 does not share the same meaning as in premise 2.
This is similar to homonymy but arises from metaphorical extension.
4
State the correction
God is 'light' metaphorically, meaning He is the source of guidance and illumination, not a physical light. Therefore, the conclusion that God is perceptible does not follow.
Metaphorical language requires interpretation, not literal inference.

Sophistry and Fallacies
