Conceptual Fallacies: Begging the Question and False Cause
Listing two key conceptual fallacies in the author's enumeration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacies of begging the question (muṣādara) and false cause in Islamic legal and theological arguments.

Core Idea
Begging the question assumes what needs proving; false cause mistakes a non-causal factor for a cause.

After verbal fallacies, the author lists conceptual fallacies. Among the most important are begging the question (al-muṣādara ʿalā al-maṭlūb al-awwal) and false cause (jaʿl mā laysa bi-ʿilla ʿilla). This lesson focuses on these two, using examples from Islamic reasoning to illustrate how they undermine arguments.

Guided Path
1
Define each fallacy
Begging the question occurs when the conclusion is assumed in one of the premises. False cause occurs when something is treated as a cause (ʿilla) without proper justification.
These are errors in the matter (premises), not the form.
2
Find begging the question in a fiqh example
Consider: 'Alcohol is forbidden because it is intoxicating, and everything intoxicating is forbidden.' This is actually valid, but if someone said: 'Alcohol is forbidden because it is prohibited by God' – that would be circular if 'forbidden' is defined by divine prohibition.
The fallacy is not always obvious; look for premises that restate the conclusion.
3
Examine false cause in a kalam argument
For example: 'The world is created; everything created has a creator; therefore the world has a creator.' This is valid. But a false cause might be: 'Prayer times are determined by the sun's position; therefore the sun causes the prayer obligation.' The sun's position is a sign, not a cause.
In usul al-fiqh, distinguishing 'illa (cause) from 'alamah (sign) is crucial.
4
Apply the concepts to a new argument
Take a statement: 'Riba is forbidden because it is unjust. Unjust transactions are forbidden. Therefore riba is forbidden.' Is this begging the question? Possibly, if 'unjust' is defined in terms of riba.
Check whether the premise adds new information or simply repeats the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ب] وأما الذي من جهة المادة وهي المقدمات فذلك من جملة ما قد مرَّ الكلام فيه من الاشتراك في اللفظ وغيره؛ فإن الغلط لا يكون واقعا إلا بسبب الاشتباه في اللفظ أو بسبب الاشتباه في المعنى. أما في اللفظ [١] فمنه ما ينشأ من نفس اللفظ كقول القائل إن الواجب إذا كان موجودًا فإما أن يكون ممكن الوجود، أو لا يكون؛ فإن كان ممكن الوجود وكل ما يمكن وجوده يمكن عدمه فالواجب مما يمكن عدمه وذلك محال. وإن لم يكن ممكن الوجود وكل ما لا يمكن وجوده فهو ممتنع؛ فالواجب ممتنع، وإنه محال أيضًا. ووجه الغلط فيه باشتراك اللفظ وهو الممكن بين الممكن الخاص والعام والعام هو اللا ممتنع والممكن بهذا المعنى لا يجب أن يكون ممكن العدم؛ إذ الواجب لذاته ممكن بهذا المعنى ؛ وبغير هذا المعنى لا يمكن أن يوصف به الواجب. [٢] ومنه ما ينشأ من صيغته نحو القابل فإن صيغته الفاعل فيظن أن القبول فعل حتى يقال إن الهيولى فاعلة على أنها قابلة؛ [٣] ومنه ما ينشأ من التصريف كلفظ المختار فإنه يمكن أن يكون فاعلا ويمكن أن يكون مفعولاً. وكذلك في قولنا ضرب زيد فإنه يمكن أن يكون زيد فاعلا ويمكن أن يكون مفعولاً لما أن المصدر يضاف تارة إلى الفاعل وتارة إلى المفعول؛ [٤] ومنه ما ينشأ من الإعجام والتشديد والتخفيف وغير ذلك على ما عرف؛ [٥] ومنه [٣٤٩] ما يكون من كون اللفظ / مجازا كما يقال إنه تعالى نور، والنور محسوس فالله تعالى محسوس؛ ولا يلزم أن يكون كذلك. وإطلاق لفظ النور على الله سبحانه بطريق المجاز. وأما في المعنى فمثل ما مرّ [٦] من سوء اعتبار الحمل [۷] والمصادرة على المطلوب الأول، [۸] وجعل ما ليس بعلة علة، [۹] وغير ذلك من تركيب المفصل، [١٠] وتفصيل المركب [١١] وأخذ اللازم مكان الملزوم ، [۱۲] وأخذ ما بالعرض مكان ما بالذات، [١٣ ] وأخذ ما بالقوة مكان ما بالفعل [١٤] وبالعكس في الأمثلة المذكورة. وما عدا تلك الأمثلة فلها من الأمثلة من كل نوع ؛ وذلك يعرف بالتأمل. [ت] ولما ظهر عليك ما يكون من أسباب الغلط الواقع في الصورة تارة وفي المادة أخرى فقد ظهر أيضًا ما يكون من كل واحدة منهما كما إذا كان القياس منحرفًا عن الصورة القياسية وهى التأليف، وعن مادتها وهي المقدمات الستة
[B] As for that which is from the aspect of matter, i.e., the premises, that is among what has already been discussed concerning verbal homonymy and other things. For error only occurs due to confusion in wording or confusion in meaning. As for wording: [1] from it is what arises from the word itself, like the claim: 'If the Necessary Being exists, then either it is possible of existence or not. If it is possible of existence, and everything possible of existence is possible of non-existence, then the Necessary Being is among that which is possible of non-existence, and that is impossible. And if it is not possible of existence, and everything not possible of existence is impossible, then the Necessary Being is impossible, and that is also impossible.' The aspect of error in it is due to the homonymy of the word 'possible' between the specific possible and the general (the general is the non-impossible). 'Possible' in this sense does not require that it be possible of non-existence; indeed, the Necessary Being in itself is possible in this sense, and in no other sense can it be described as possible. [2] From it is what arises from its form, like the word 'al-qabil' (the receiver): its form is fa'il (active participle), so one thinks that receiving is an action, such that it is said that hyle is acting while it is receptive. [3] From it is what arises from conjugation, like the word 'al-mukhtar' — it can be an active agent or a passive object. Likewise in our saying 'Zayd was struck' (daraba Zayd), Zayd can be the subject (striker) or the object (struck) because the verbal noun is sometimes attributed to the subject and sometimes to the object. [4] From it is what arises from diacritical marks, gemination, lightening, and other things as is known. [5] From it is what arises from the word being metaphorical, as when it is said that He (exalted) is light, and light is perceptible, so God (exalted) is perceptible; but that does not necessarily follow. The application of the word 'light' to God (glorified) is by way of metaphor. As for meaning, it is like what has passed: [6] ill-considered predication, [7] begging the question (petitio principii), [8] taking what is not a cause as a cause, [9] and other things like composition of the divided, [10] division of the composed, [11] taking the necessary along with the necessary-less, [12] taking the accidental for the essential, [13] taking the potential for the actual, [14] and the reverse in the mentioned examples. Apart from those examples, there are examples for each type; that is known by reflection. [T] When it has become clear to you what are the causes of error occurring sometimes in form and other times in matter, it has also become clear what results from both together, as when the syllogism deviates from the syllogistic form, which is composition, and from its matter, which are the six premises.
Source pages p. 599

Commentary
This passage continues the comprehensive summary of fallacies, turning to material errors. Material fallacies are divided into two broad categories: those caused by confusion in words (al-ashbāh fī al-lafẓ) and those caused by confusion in meaning (al-ashbāh fī al-maʿnā). The author illustrates verbal fallacies with several examples. The first is the fallacy of homonymy (ishtirāk), using the word 'possible' (mumkin) as applied to the Necessary Being (al-wājib). The fallacy arises because 'possible' can mean either 'contingent' (possible of existence and non-existence) or 'non-impossible' (which includes the Necessary Being). The argument incorrectly concludes that the Necessary Being is impossible by confusing the two senses. Secondly, the fallacy of form (ṣīgha) is shown with 'al-qābil' (receiver): its active participle form suggests that receiving is an action, whereas in reality hyle (matter) is passive. Thirdly, the fallacy of conjugation (taṣrīf) is illustrated with 'al-mukhtār' which can be active or passive, and the ambiguous phrase 'ḍaraba Zayd' can mean either 'Zayd struck' or 'Zayd was struck'. Fourthly, errors from diacritics (iʿjām), gemination (tashdīd), and lightening (takhfīf) are mentioned. Fifthly, the fallacy of metaphor (majāz) is seen when 'light' is applied to God; one might mistakenly infer that because light is sensible, God is sensible, but the term is used metaphorically. The passage then turns to conceptual fallacies, listing them by number: [6] ill-considered predication (sūʾ iʿtibār al-ḥaml), [7] begging the question (al-muṣādara ʿalā al-maṭlūb al-awwal), [8] false cause (jaʿl mā laysa bi-ʿilla ʿilla), [9] composition of the divided (tarkīb al-mufaṣṣal), [10] division of the composed (tafṣīl al-murakkab), [11] taking the necessary along with the necessary-less (akhdh al-lāzim makān al-malzūm), [12] taking the accidental for the essential (akhdh mā bi-al-ʿaraḍ makān mā bi-al-dhāt), [13] taking the potential for the actual (akhdh mā bi-al-quwwa makān mā bi-al-fiʿl), and [14] the reverse. The author notes that there are examples for each type and that they can be known through reflection. Finally, a concluding remark [T] states that when errors of form and matter are understood, errors combining both are also evident, such as a syllogism that deviates both in its structure (composition) and in its matter (the six kinds of premises).

Sophistry and Fallacies

Fallacies from Words and Meanings: A Classical Classification
Author enumerates types of fallacies arising from wording and from meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between verbal and conceptual fallacies and identify the specific subtypes mentioned by the author.

Core Idea
Fallacies are either in wording or in meaning; knowing the difference guards your reasoning.

In this passage, the author systematizes the types of fallacies (mughalata) that undermine arguments. He distinguishes fallacies arising from ambiguous or misleading words from those rooted in flawed logical operations. By studying these categories, you will learn to spot common errors in Islamic theological and legal debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Main Categories
Notice that the passage begins with a detailed example of a verbal fallacy (equivocation) and then lists additional verbal types, followed by conceptual fallacies introduced with 'wa amma fi al-ma'na'.
The transition from verbal to conceptual is explicit.
2
Analyze the Equivocation Example
The term 'mumkin' has a specific sense (possible of non-existence) and a general sense (non-impossible). The Necessary Existent is possible only in the general sense; the argument that it must be impossible is fallacious because it shifts between these senses.
This is a classic case of ishtirak al-lafz.
3
List the Five Verbal Fallacies
The passage enumerates: from morphological form (sigha), from inflection (tasrif), from diacritical marks/doubling/lightening (i'jam/tashdid/takhfif), and from metaphor (majaz). For each, understand how the form misleads the listener.
These are specific to Arabic morphology and rhetoric.
4
Recognize the Conceptual Fallacies
The author lists several by number: bad predication, begging the question, false cause, composition of the separate, division of the composite, taking necessary for necessitated, accidental for essential, potential for actual, and their converses.
These are standard logical fallacies found in many traditions.
5
Reflect on Application in Islamic Sciences
Consider how these fallacies appear in real debates, such as the majaz fallacy in discussing divine attributes or the takhif/musharaka fallacies in legal reasoning.
Practice identifying them in actual arguments.

عدمه فالواجب مما يمكن عدمه وذلك محال. وإن لم يكن ممكن الوجود وكل ما لا يمكن وجوده فهو ممتنع؛ فالواجب ممتنع، وإنه محال أيضًا. ووجه الغلط فيه باشتراك اللفظ وهو الممكن بين الممكن الخاص والعام والعام هو اللا ممتنع والممكن بهذا المعنى لا يجب أن يكون ممكن العدم؛ إذ الواجب لذاته ممكن بهذا المعنى؛ وبغير هذا المعنى لا يمكن أن يوصف به الواجب. [٢] ومنه ما ينشأ من صيغته نحو القابل فإن صيغته الفاعل فيظن أن القبول فعل حتى يقال إن الهيولى فاعلة على أنها قابلة؛ [٣] ومنه ما ينشأ من التصريف كلفظ المختار فإنه يمكن أن يكون فاعلا ويمكن أن يكون مفعولاً. وكذلك في قولنا ضرب زيد فإنه يمكن أن يكون زيد فاعلا ويمكن أن يكون مفعولاً لما أن المصدر يضاف تارة إلى الفاعل وتارة إلى المفعول؛ [٤] ومنه ما ينشأ من الإعجام والتشديد والتخفيف وغير ذلك على ما عرف؛ [٥] ومنه ما يكون من كون اللفظ مجازا كما يقال إنه تعالى نور، والنور محسوس فالله تعالى محسوس؛ ولا يلزم أن يكون كذلك. وإطلاق لفظ النور على الله سبحانه بطريق المجاز. وأما في المعنى فمثل ما مرّ [٦] من سوء اعتبار الحمل [۷] والمصادرة على المطلوب الأول، [۸] وجعل ما ليس بعلة علة، [۹] وغير ذلك من تركيب المفصل، [١٠] وتفصيل المركب [١١] وأخذ اللازم مكان الملزوم، [۱۲] وأخذ ما بالعرض مكان ما بالذات، [۱۳] وأخذ ما بالقوة مكان ما بالفعل [١٤] وبالعكس في الأمثلة المذكورة. وما عدا تلك الأمثلة فلها من الأمثلة من كل نوع؛ وذلك يعرف بالتأمل.
Its non-existence is impossible for the Necessary, for that which can be non-existent is impossible. And if it is not possible of existence—and everything whose existence is impossible is impossible—then the Necessary is impossible, and that is also impossible. The error in it is due to equivocation (ishtirak al-lafz) in the word 'possible' (mumkin) between the specific possible and the general possible. The general is the non-impossible, and the possible in this sense does not have to be possible of non-existence, since the Necessary in itself is possible in this sense; and in other than this sense the Necessary cannot be described by it. [2] Among them is what arises from its morphological form (sigha), like 'al-qabil' (the receiver), for its form is the active participle, so one thinks that receiving is an act, such that it is said that matter (hayula) is an agent (fa'ila) while it is a receiver. [3] Among them is what arises from inflection (tasrif), like the word 'al-mukhtar' (the chosen/choosing), for it can be an agent or a patient. Similarly in our saying 'daraba Zayd' ('Zayd struck'), Zayd can be the agent or the patient, because the verbal noun (masdar) is sometimes attributed to the agent and sometimes to the patient. [4] Among them is what arises from pointing (i'jam), doubling (tashdid), lightening (takhfif), and other things as is known. [5] Among them is what arises from the word being metaphorical (majaz), such as when it is said that He, exalted, is light (nur), and light is sensible, so God is sensible; but it is not necessary that it be so. The use of the word 'light' for God is metaphorical. As for in meaning, it is like what has passed: [6] bad consideration of predication (su' i'tibar al-haml), [7] begging the question (musadara 'ala al-matlub al-awwal), [8] taking what is not a cause as a cause, [9] composition of the separate (tarkib al-mufassal), [10] division of the composite (tafsil al-murakkab), [11] taking the necessary (lazim) in place of the necessitated (malzum), [12] taking the accidental (bi'l-'arad) in place of the essential (bi'l-dhat), [13] taking the potential (bi'l-quwwa) in place of the actual (bi'l-fi'l), [14] and the reverse in the mentioned examples. For other than those examples, there are examples of each type, and that is known by reflection.
Source pages p. 599

Commentary
This passage classifies fallacies (mughalata) into two main categories: those arising from wording (lafz) and those arising from meaning (ma'na). The author begins with an example of a verbal fallacy due to equivocation (ishtirak al-lafz) on the term 'mumkin' (possible). The fallacy arises because 'mumkin' has a specific sense (requiring non-existence possibility) and a general sense (simply non-impossible). The Necessary Existent (wajib al-wujud) is possible in the general sense but not the specific, so an argument that attempts to prove impossibility based on the specific sense is fallacious. Then the author lists five types of verbal fallacies: (1) from morphological form (sigha), e.g., 'al-qabil' (the receiver) has the active particle form, leading one to think receiving is an act; (2) from inflection (tasrif), e.g., 'al-mukhtar' can mean 'choosing' or 'chosen', and 'daraba Zayd' can be active or passive depending on the attribute of the masdar; (3) from diacritical marks (i'jam), doubling (tashdid), and lightening (takhfif) which change meaning; (4) from metaphorical usage (majaz), e.g., calling God 'light' leads to the false inference that God is sensible, but the term is metaphorical. After verbal fallacies, the author lists conceptual fallacies (fi al-ma'na) by number: bad consideration of predication, begging the question, false cause, composition of the separate, division of the composite, taking the necessary for the necessitated, accidentally for essentially, potential for actual, and their converses. These are standard fallacies in the logical tradition. The passage concludes by noting that further examples for each type can be found through reflection.

Fallacies can be classified as verbal (due to ambiguous wording) or conceptual (due to flawed reasoning); recognizing them is essential for sound argumentation in Islamic sciences.

Classification of False Propositions in Material Fallacies
Classification of false propositions and their deceptive resemblance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between entirely false (kadhib bi'l-kull) and partly false (kadhib fi'l-juz') propositions and identify whether their resemblance to truth arises from true or illusory subsumption.

Core Idea
False propositions deceive by resembling truth through subsumption under universals.

In material fallacies, the premises may be false but resemble true ones in meaning. The author classifies these false propositions and explains how they achieve this resemblance. Understanding this classification helps you recognize deceptive arguments in Islamic legal and theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of false propositions
From the passage, list the three categories: entirely false (kadhib bi'l-kull), false in part (kadhib fi'l-juz'), and false in aspect (fi jihat). Note the defining feature of each.
Only the first two are elaborated with examples.
2
Understand the concept of subsumption (indiraj)
The resemblance of an entirely false proposition to a true one occurs when it is subsumed under a universal. Distinguish true subsumption (indiraj haqiqi) from illusory subsumption (indiraj wahmi). In true subsumption, the particular genuinely falls under the universal; in illusory, it only seems to.
The universal can be a genus, differentia, or accident.
3
Analyze the examples for true subsumption
The example: blackness gathers the sight (true), so one thinks all colors gather the sight, and whiteness is a color, so whiteness gathers the sight. The subsumption of whiteness under color is real, but the premise that all colors gather the sight is false. Thus the false proposition resembles truth via true subsumption.
Identify the universal (color) and the property (gathering sight).
4
Analyze the examples for false in part
Examples: laughing applies only to humans, but if applied to animal, it is false for some animals. Similarly, seeing a small fluid leads to the generalization 'every fluid is small'. The error is applying a particular judgment to a broader universal.
Also the example of the necessary (lazim) being wrongly equated to the necessitated (malzum).
5
Apply the classification to a new case
Given a proposition like 'All animals are rational', determine its type. It is false in part because only humans are rational, so the judgment fails for some animals.
Practice with other Islamic science examples to solidify recognition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ت] ولما ظهر عليك ما يكون من أسباب الغلط الواقع في الصورة تارة وفي المادة أخرى فقد ظهر أيضًا ما يكون من كل واحدة منهما كما إذا كان القياس منحرفًا عن الصورة القياسية وهى التأليف، وعن مادتها وهي المقدمات الستة في كونها منتجة. فإن ما يكون كذلك كان مشتملا على الاشتباه بحسب الصورة والمادة. والتأليف قد يكون بطريق الاستقامة، وقد لا يكون. والمقدمات قد تكون بطريق الصحة كما إذا كانت صادقة لا ريب فيها، وقد لا تكون بل تكون كاذبة مثلا. والكاذبة قد تكون مشتبهة بالصادقة من جهة المعنى؛ وحينئذ إما أن يكون الكاذب كاذبا بالكل وهو الذي لا يصدق الحكم على شيء من موضوعه البتة، وإما أن يكون كاذبًا في الجزء، وإما أن لا يكون كاذبًا فيهما بل في جهة. أما ما يكون كاذبًا بالكل فمشابهته مع الصادق إنما يكون باندراجها تحت كلي إما حقيقة أو وهما، والكلية إما جنس أو فصل أو عارض. أما الاندراج الحقيقي فمثل أن يحكم أن كل بياض جامع للبصر لما أن السواد جامع للبصر فيتوهم أن جميع السواد للبصر إنما هو لكونه لونا؛ والبياض لون فيثبت له هذا الحكم. وأما الاندراج الوهمي فمثل أن يحكم بالوهم أن الهيولى أو العقل مثلاً مشارًا إلى جهته بسبب أن الجسم مشارًا إلى جهته ليتوهم اندراج الصادق والكاذب منهما تحت المحسوس. ومن هذا القبيل جميع الوهميات الكاذبة التي مرَّ ذكرها. وأما ما يكون كاذبا بالجزء فمنه ما يكون الحكم على جزئي فيحمل على الكلي الذي فوقه كالضاحك الذي لا يصدق إلا على الإنسان؛ فيحمل على الحيوان فيكون الحكم كاذبا في البعض منه؛ إذ بعضه ليس بضاحك. وقد مرّ من قبل ما يكون من هذا القبيل وهو أن يرى شيئًا سيالًا أصغر هو كذا توهم أن كل سيال أصغر كذا. وكذلك الحكم على لازم الشيء بما يصدق على الشيء فإن اللازم إذا كان محمولاً على شيء وشيء آخر محمول على كل ذلك الملزوم يوهم أن ذلك اللازم مساوٍ لملزومه حتى يصح أن يحمل على كله ما يحمل على كل الملزوم؛ وإنما الواجب صحة الحكم على بعض اللازم فقط. ومن الكاذب في الجزء ما يصح
[T] And when it has become clear to you what are the causes of error (asbab al-ghalat) occurring in form (sura) sometimes and in matter (madda) other times, it has also become clear what arises from each of them, such as when the syllogism (qiyas) deviates from the syllogistic form, which is the composition (ta'lif), and from its matter, which are the six premises in their productiveness. So what is like that contains confusion (ishtibah) in both form and matter. Composition may be in a correct way or not. Premises may be in a correct way, as when they are certain and true, or they may not be, but false, for example. False ones may resemble true ones in meaning. Then either the false is entirely false (kadhib bi'l-kull), which is that the judgment does not apply to anything of its subject at all, or it is false in part (kadhib fi'l-juz'), or it is not false in both but in aspect (jiha). As for what is entirely false, its resemblance to the true is only by subsumption (indiraj) under a universal, either truly or illusorily, and the universal is either a genus, a differentia, or an accident. As for true subsumption, like judging that every whiteness gathers the sight, because blackness gathers the sight, so one thinks that all blackness gathers the sight solely because it is a color; and whiteness is a color, so this judgment is established for it. As for illusory subsumption, like judging by illusion that matter (hayula) or intellect is pointed to by its direction, because body is pointed to by its direction, so that one imagines that the true and false are subsumed under the sensible. Of this kind are all the false illusions (wahmiyyat kadhiba) mentioned before. As for what is false in part, among it is when the judgment is on a particular (juz'i) and is applied to the universal above it, like 'laughing' (dahik) which applies only to humans; it is applied to animal, so the judgment is false for some of it, since some animals are not laughing. Previously there passed what is of this kind: seeing something fluid and small and thinking that every fluid is small like that. Likewise, judging on the necessary (lazim) of a thing based on what applies to the thing. For if the necessary is predicated of something and another thing is predicated of all that necessitated (malzum), one imagines that the necessary is equivalent to its necessitated, so that what is predicated of all the necessitated can be predicated of all the necessary; but the correct judgment is only on some of the necessary. And of the false in part is what is valid...
Source pages p. 599

Commentary
This passage discusses the material side of fallacies, specifically the classification of false premises (mukhtalitu al-qadaya) that resemble true ones. The author first recaps that errors occur either in form (sura) or matter (madda). In material fallacies, the premises are false but resemble truth in meaning. The author divides false propositions into three types: entirely false (kadhib bi'l-kull), false in part (kadhib fi'l-juz'), and false in aspect (fi jihat). For the entirely false, the resemblance to truth comes from being subsumed under a universal (kulli) either truly (haqiqi) or illusorily (wahmi). The example of true subsumption: because blackness gathers the sight (a true property of blackness), one might think that all colors gather the sight, and since whiteness is a color, it must also gather the sight. But this is false because 'gathering the sight' is not true of all colors. The subsumption under 'color' is real, but the premise that all colors have that property is false. Illusory subsumption: because bodies are perceptible by sense, one might imagine that matter or intellect are also perceptible, but this is false. The false in part occurs when a judgment that applies to a particular (juz'i) is applied to a universal containing it, e.g., 'laughing' applies only to humans, but if applied to 'animal', it is false for some animals. Another example: seeing a small fluid thing leads to the universal claim that all fluid things are small. Also, taking a necessary (lazim) as equivalent to its necessitated (malzum) leads to false generalization; e.g., if a property belongs to all of a thing, it might wrongly be assumed to belong to all its necessary concomitants. The passage ends while discussing false in part.

Key Terms
كاذب بالكل
كاذب في الجزء
اندراج حقيقي
اندراج وهمي
لازم

B) False in part (kadhib fi'l-juz')
C) False in aspect (fi jihat)

Key Takeaway
False propositions in material fallacies are classified as entirely false, false in part, or false in aspect, and their deceptive resemblance to truth arises from either true or illusory subsumption under a universal.

Sophistry and Fallacies
intermediate
12 min
Three Types of False Premises and Their Resemblance to True Ones
Author classifies false premises into three types with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between entirely false, false in part, and false in respect premises in any given argument and identify how each mimics a true premise.

Core Idea
False premises mislead by resembling truth; identifying their type is key to refuting sophistry.

Not every false premise is a bare lie. Some are entirely false, others are true in part but misapplied, and still others confuse what is accidental with what is essential. In this lesson, you will learn to recognise these three types of false premises as classified by classical logicians. This skill is vital for dissecting arguments in Islamic sciences, where false analogies or overly broad claims often masquerade as sound reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise under scrutiny
Extract the single proposition that is claimed to be true. For example, 'Every whiteness collects sight' or 'All animals laugh'. This is the kadhib (false premise) in question.
2
Check the subject and predicate relationship
Determine whether the predicate is true of all individuals of the subject, some, or none. If it is true of none, it is entirely false (kadhib bi al-kull). If true of some but not all, it is false in part (kadhib fi al-juz'). If the truth depends on a condition, aspect, or accidental attribute, it is false in respect (kadhib fi jihha).
The passage gives ‘laughing’ predicated of ‘animal’ as false in part, since only humans (a subset) laugh.
3
Determine the mode of resemblance
For entirely false premises, ask how it resembles truth: through real or imaginary subsumption under a universal. For false in part, ask if the mistake is overgeneralising from a partial observation or confusing a necessary concomitant with the subject. For false in respect, check if accidental is taken as essential, or potential as actual.
Real subsumption: whiteness wrongly given a property of blackness because both are colours. Imaginary: matter thought to be pointable because body is.
4
Classify and verify with an Islamic science example
Take an example from fiqh, kalam, or tafsir that fits one of the three types. For instance, ‘Every intoxicant is forbidden; this fruit juice may intoxicate if left too long; so it is forbidden’ – here the false premise is ‘this fruit juice is an intoxicant essentially’ (false in respect if intoxication is accidental). Verify which type matches.
This step connects the abstract classification to real scholarly argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في كونها منتجة. فإن ما يكون كذلك كان مشتملا على الاشتباه بحسب الصورة والمادة. والتأليف قد يكون بطريق الاستقامة، وقد لا يكون. والمقدمات قد تكون بطريق الصحة كما إذا كانت صادقة لا ريب فيها ، وقد لا تكون بل تكون كاذبة مثلا . والكاذبة قد تكون مشتبهة بالصادقة من جهة المعنى؛ وحينئذ إما أن يكون الكاذب كاذبا بالكل وهو الذي لا يصدق الحكم على شيء من موضوعه البتة، وإما أن يكون كاذبًا في الجزء ، وإما أن لا يكون كاذبًا فيهما بل في جهة. أما ما يكون كاذبًا بالكل فمشابهته مع الصادق إنما يكون باندراجها تحت كلي إما حقيقة أو وهما والكلية إما جنس أو فصل أو عارض. أما الاندراج الحقيقي فمثل أن يحكم أن كل بياض جامع للبصر لما أن السواد جامع للبصر فيتوهم أن جميع السواد للبصر إنما هو لكونه لونا؛ والبياض لون فيثبت له هذا الحكم. وأما الاندراج الوهمي فمثل أن يحكم بالوهم أن الهيولى أو العقل مثلاً مشارًا إلى جهته بسبب أن الجسم مشارًا إلى جهته ليتوهم اندراج الصادق والكاذب منهما تحت المحسوس. ومن هذا القبيل جميع الوهميات الكاذبة التي مرَّ ذكرها. وأما ما يكون كاذبا بالجزء فمنه ما يكون الحكم على جزئي فيحمل على الكلي الذي فوقه كالضاحك الذي لا يصدق إلا على الإنسان؛ فيحمل على الحيوان فيكون الحكم كاذبا في البعض منه؛ إذ بعضه ليس بضاحك. وقد مرّ من قبل ما يكون من هذا القبيل وهو أن يرى شيئًا سيالًا أصغر هو كذا توهم أن كل سيال أصغر كذا. وكذلك الحكم على لازم الشيء بما يصدق على الشيء فإن اللازم إذا كان محمولاً على شيء وشيء آخر محمول على كل ذلك الملزوم يوهم أن ذلك اللازم مساوٍ لملزومه حتى يصح أن يحمل على كله ما يحمل على كل الملزوم؛ وإنما الواجب صحة الحكم على بعض اللازم فقط. ومن الكاذب في الجزء ما يصح الحكم على موضوع شرط أو في حال أو في وقت فيؤخذ بدون ذلك الشرط مثلا أو في كل وقت أو في وقت آخر دون ذلك الوقت. وأما ما يكون كاذبا في جهة الحكم فمثل ما إذا أخذ بالعرض مكان ما بالذات كما يقال إن السقامنيا مبردة بالذات؛ وإنما هي بالعرض لإزالتها المسخّن بالذات وهو الصفراء على ما عرف. وكذلك إذا أخذ ما بالقوة مكان ما بالفعل أو بالعكس كما مرّ.
In its being productive. For what is like that is inclusive of confusion with respect to form and matter. Composition may be in the manner of correctness, and may not be. Premises may be in the manner of truth, as when they are true with no doubt, and may not be, but rather be false, for example. The false may resemble the true in terms of meaning; and then the false is either entirely false, which is that the judgment is not true of any of its subject at all, or false in part, or not false in both but in respect. As for what is entirely false, its resemblance to the true is only by its subsumption under a universal either truly or falsely, and the universal is either genus or differentia or accident. As for real subsumption, like judging that every whiteness is sight-collecting because blackness is sight-collecting, so one imagines that all blackness's being sight-collecting is only because it is a color; and whiteness is a color, so this judgment is affirmed for it. As for imaginary subsumption, like judging erroneously that matter or intellect, for example, is pointable to in its direction because body is pointable to in its direction, so that one imagines the subsumption of the true and false from them under the sensible. Of this kind are all the false imaginal premises that have passed. As for what is false in part, from it is that the judgment is upon a particular and is predicated of the universal above it, like 'laughing' which is true only of human; so it is predicated of animal, so the judgment is false in some of it, since some of it is not laughing. And there has passed before what is of this kind, namely that he sees a flowing thing smaller being such-and-such, so he imagines that every flowing smaller is such-and-such. Likewise the judgment upon a necessary concomitant of a thing with what is true of the thing, for the necessary concomitant, if it is predicated of something and another thing is predicated of every that substratum, it suggests that that necessary concomitant is equal to its substratum such that it is correct to predicate of all of it what is predicated of all the substratum; but the necessary is the correctness of the judgment on only some of the necessary concomitant. And of the false in part is what is correct to judge upon a subject conditionally or in a state or in a time, but it is taken without that condition, for example, or in every time or in another time apart from that time. And as for what is false in respect of the judgment, it is like when accidental is taken instead of essential, as it is said that scammony is cooling essentially; but it is accidentally cooling because it removes the essentially hot, which is bile, as is known. And likewise when potential is taken instead of actual, or vice versa, as has passed.
Source pages p. 600

Commentary
This passage defines the three types of false premises that can lead to sophistical arguments. The author begins by stating that a productive argument that includes confusion can be due to form or matter. He then focuses on matter (premises). False premises may resemble true ones. He divides false premises into three kinds: entirely false (kadhib bil-kull), false in part (kadhib fi al-juz'), and false in respect (kadhib fi jihha). For each, he explains how they resemble true ones. For entirely false, resemblance occurs through subsumption under a universal, either real or imaginary. Real subsumption is when a property is wrongly transferred from one species to another under the same genus, like attributing sight-collecting to whiteness because blackness has it. Imaginary subsumption is when something is imagined to be similar based on a false analogy, like thinking matter or intellect is pointable because body is. For false in part, the judgment is true of some but not all of the subject, like predicating 'laughing' of animal (only humans laugh). Also, mistaken generalization from partial observation, like thinking every flowing thing is small. Another subtype is confusing the necessary concomitant with its substratum, assuming what holds for the substratum holds for the concomitant. Also, truth conditional on time/state is taken unconditionally. For false in respect, the accidental is taken as essential, e.g., scammony is said to be cooling essentially, but it is only accidentally cooling by removing heat. Also, potential is taken as actual or vice versa. These distinctions help identify fallacies in reasoning.

اشتباه
اندراج
بالعرض

Sophistry and Fallacies

First Dialectical Trick: Illicit Universalization on Non-Existence
First sophistical argument with its refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and refute the fallacy of illicit universalization in a kalam argument about non-existence.

Core Idea
A particular claim about bodies cannot be universalized without violating logic.

Early scholars sometimes used clever but fallacious arguments to defend their positions. The first famous trick claims that 'some body is not receptive to non-existence' and then illicitly generalizes to all bodies. The refutation uses a first-figure syllogism to expose the absurdity. This lesson teaches you to detect such hasty universalizations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial claim
The trick begins with 'some body is not receptive to non-existence' (بعض الجسم لا يكون قابلا للعدم). This is a particular claim about at least one body.
The claimant tries to build on this limited statement.
2
See the universal inference attempted
The opponent argues: 'if that were true, then whenever something is a body, it would be receptive to non-existence' (وإلا لكان كلما كان الشيء جسما كان قابلا للعدم). This extrapolates from 'some' to 'all'.
This is the illicit step.
3
Observe the first-figure syllogism
To expose the fallacy, the author constructs a syllogism in the first figure: 'Whenever a thing is a body and not receptive to non-existence, it is a body' leads to 'it is receptive to non-existence'. The conclusion is impossible (محال).
First figure: middle term is subject of major and predicate of minor.
4
Formulate the fallacy
The error is treating a particular affirmative as universal. From 'some S is P' you cannot infer 'all S is P' without further evidence.
This fallacy is common in theological debates about eternal attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في النكت المشهورة في باب المغالطات عند السلف ]
وأما النكت المشهورة في باب المغالطات عند السلف فهي هذه: [أ] أما الأولى فأن يقال بعض الجسم لا يكون قابلا للعدم وإلا لكان كلما كان الشيء جسما كان قابلا للعدم؛ والتالي محال فإنه إذا صدق كان ينظم مع المقدمة الصادقة في نفس الأمر وهي قولنا كلما كان الشيء جسما وليس قابلا للعدم فهو جسم قياسًا منتجا من الشكل الأول كقولنا كلما كان الشيء جسما وليس قابلا للعدم فهو قابل للعدم؛ وإنه محال. [ب] وأما الثانية فإن يقال إن الفلك مركب من أجزاء غير متجزئة فإنه يصدق قولنا قد يكون إذا كان الفلك جسما فهو مركب من أجزاء غير متجزئة من الشكل الثالث؛ والأوسط مجموع طرفيه . ثم يقال لكنه جسم دائمًا فيلزم أن يكون مركبًا من أجزاء غير متجزئة. [ت] وأما الثالثة فيقال الإمكان لا يكون علة للاحتياج إلى المؤثر؛ إذ كل ما هو علة للاحتياج إلى المؤثر فهو ضروري أن يكون علة للاحتياج إلى المؤثر ولا شيء من الإمكان بضروري أن يكون علة للاحتياج إلى المؤثر شيء من الإمكان بعلة للاحتياج إلى المؤثر. [ث] وأما الرابعة فيقال إن الجسم من حيث هو الجسم لا يمكن أن يكون موجودًا فإنه إذا كان موجودًا فإما أن يكون موجودًا في الخارج ؛ وإنه هو الجزئي حينئذ لما أنه يمكن أن يشار إليه إشارة حسية، وإما أن يكون موجودًا في الذهن؛ وإنه هو الجزئي أيضًا لما
Section: On the famous points in the chapter of fallacies according to the Salaf. And the famous points in the chapter of fallacies according to the Salaf are these: [A] The first: that it is said: Some body is not receptive to non-existence; otherwise, whenever a thing is a body, it would be receptive to non-existence; but the consequent is impossible, for if it were true, it would combine with the true premise in itself, which is our statement: Whenever a thing is a body and not receptive to non-existence, it is a body, producing a syllogism in the first figure, like our statement: Whenever a thing is a body and not receptive to non-existence, it is receptive to non-existence; and that is impossible. [B] The second: that it is said: The celestial sphere is composed of indivisible parts; for it is true that it may be that if the celestial sphere is a body, then it is composed of indivisible parts, from the third figure; and the middle term is the sum of its two extremes. Then it is said: but it is always a body, so it follows that it is composed of indivisible parts. [C] The third: that it is said: Possibility is not a cause of need for an effect; for everything that is a cause of need for an effect is necessarily a cause of need for an effect, and nothing of possibility is necessarily a cause of need for an effect. [D] The fourth: that it is said: The body insofar as it is body cannot exist; for if it exists, then either it exists externally, and then it is the particular since it can be pointed to sensibly, or it exists in the mind, and then it is also the particular, since
Source pages p. 601

Commentary
This section presents four famous dialectical tricks used by early scholars (salaf). Each is a problematic argument that leads to a contradiction or absurdity. The first trick concerns the impossibility of non-existence for bodies. The argument suggests that if some body cannot cease to exist, then all bodies cannot cease to exist, which is false because bodies can change. The argument is shown to produce a syllogism in the first figure with an impossible conclusion. The second tricks concerns the composition of the celestial sphere (falak) from indivisible parts (juz' la yatajazza). The argument tries to prove that the sphere is composed of indivisible parts using a third-figure syllogism. The third trick is about possibility (imkan) and its relation to a cause (mu'aththir). It states that possibility is not a cause for needing an effect, because if it were, it would be necessary, but possibility is not necessary. The fourth trick argues that body qua body cannot exist, because if it exists it is either external (particular) or mental (also particular), so it cannot be universal. These are classical sophisms that challenge the logician's ability to detect fallacies. The author presents them as examples of mudghalat (fallacies) to be recognized.

نكت
مغالطات
الشكل الأول

Key Takeaway

Sophistry and Fallacies
intermediate
5 min
Second Dialectical Trick: Proving Indivisible Parts from the Sphere
Second sophistical argument: proving indivisible parts of the sphere

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy of composition when arguing that the celestial sphere is composed of indivisible parts using a third-figure syllogism.

Core Idea
A conditional statement from the third figure does not prove factual composition.

The second trick attempts to prove that the celestial sphere (al-falak) is composed of indivisible atoms (juz' la yatajazza). It uses a conditional premise in the third figure to infer this. The refutation likely challenges the truth of that premise. This lesson helps you recognize when a syllogistic form is misapplied to a metaphysical claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target conclusion
The trick aims to show: 'the celestial sphere is composed of indivisible parts' (الفلك مركب من أجزاء غير متجزئة).
This was a common atomist position in kalam.
2
Examine the conditional premise
The argument presents a conditional: 'if the celestial sphere is a body, then it may be composed of indivisible parts' (قد يكون إذا كان الفلك جسما فهو مركب من أجزاء غير متجزئة). This is a merely possible claim, not a necessary truth.
The conditional is in the third figure, where the middle term is the sum of the two extremes.
3
Add the minor premise
Then it is said: 'but it is always a body' (لكنه جسم دائمًا). This affirms the antecedent.
This is a categorical statement.
4
Check validity
From the conditional and the minor, via modus ponens, the conclusion follows: 'it is composed of indivisible parts'. But the conditional itself is not proven; it only says 'may be', not 'is'. So the argument is not sound.
The fallacy is treating a possible conditional as a necessary one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[ب] وأما الثانية فإن يقال إن الفلك مركب من أجزاء غير متجزئة فإنه يصدق قولنا قد يكون إذا كان الفلك جسما فهو مركب من أجزاء غير متجزئة من الشكل الثالث؛ والأوسط مجموع طرفيه . ثم يقال لكنه جسم دائمًا فيلزم أن يكون مركبًا من أجزاء غير متجزئة. [ت] وأما الثالثة فيقال الإمكان لا يكون علة للاحتياج إلى المؤثر؛ إذ كل ما هو علة للاحتياج إلى المؤثر فهو ضروري أن يكون علة للاحتياج إلى المؤثر ولا شيء من الإمكان بضروري أن يكون علة للاحتياج إلى المؤثر شيء من الإمكان بعلة للاحتياج إلى المؤثر. [ث] وأما الرابعة فيقال إن الجسم من حيث هو الجسم لا يمكن أن يكون موجودًا فإنه إذا كان موجودًا فإما أن يكون موجودًا في الخارج ؛ وإنه هو الجزئي حينئذ لما أنه يمكن أن يشار إليه إشارة حسية، وإما أن يكون موجودًا في الذهن؛ وإنه هو الجزئي أيضًا لما
Section: On the famous points in the chapter of fallacies according to the Salaf. And the famous points in the chapter of fallacies according to the Salaf are these: [A] The first: that it is said: Some body is not receptive to non-existence; otherwise, whenever a thing is a body, it would be receptive to non-existence; but the consequent is impossible, for if it were true, it would combine with the true premise in itself, which is our statement: Whenever a thing is a body and not receptive to non-existence, it is a body, producing a syllogism in the first figure, like our statement: Whenever a thing is a body and not receptive to non-existence, it is receptive to non-existence; and that is impossible. [B] The second: that it is said: The celestial sphere is composed of indivisible parts; for it is true that it may be that if the celestial sphere is a body, then it is composed of indivisible parts, from the third figure; and the middle term is the sum of its two extremes. Then it is said: but it is always a body, so it follows that it is composed of indivisible parts. [C] The third: that it is said: Possibility is not a cause of need for an effect; for everything that is a cause of need for an effect is necessarily a cause of need for an effect, and nothing of possibility is necessarily a cause of need for an effect. [D] The fourth: that it is said: The body insofar as it is body cannot exist; for if it exists, then either it exists externally, and then it is the particular since it can be pointed to sensibly, or it exists in the mind, and then it is also the particular, since
Source pages p. 601

Commentary
This section presents four famous dialectical tricks used by early scholars (salaf). Each is a problematic argument that leads to a contradiction or absurdity. The first trick concerns the impossibility of non-existence for bodies. The argument suggests that if some body cannot cease to exist, then all bodies cannot cease to exist, which is false because bodies can change. The argument is shown to produce a syllogism in the first figure with an impossible conclusion. The second tricks concerns the composition of the celestial sphere (falak) from indivisible parts (juz' la yatajazza). The argument tries to prove that the sphere is composed of indivisible parts using a third-figure syllogism. The third trick is about possibility (imkan) and its relation to a cause (mu'aththir). It states that possibility is not a cause for needing an effect, because if it were, it would be necessary, but possibility is not necessary. The fourth trick argues that body qua body cannot exist, because if it exists it is either external (particular) or mental (also particular), so it cannot be universal. These are classical sophisms that challenge the logician's ability to detect fallacies. The author presents them as examples of mudghalat (fallacies) to be recognized.

Key Terms
الشكل الثالث
الأوسط مجموع طرفيه
قد يكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What makes the second nukta's major premise weak?
A) It is in the third figure
B) It says 'قد يكون' (it may be) instead of a categorical assertion
C) The middle term is not properly distributed
D) The minor premise is false

Key Takeaway
The second trick fails because it uses a possible conditional as a necessary one to derive a metaphysical claim about composition.

Third Dialectical Trick: Denying Possibility as a Cause of Need
Third sophistical argument: possibility not a cause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy of denying a necessary connection between possibility and causation in kalam arguments.

Core Idea
If possibility cannot be a cause, then contingency cannot explain need for a creator.

The third trick argues that 'possibility (imkan) is not a cause of need for an effect (mu'aththir)'. It uses a syllogism: whatever is a cause of need is necessarily a cause of need; but no possibility is necessarily such; therefore possibility is not a cause. This attack targets the standard kalam proof that contingent beings need a cause. The fallacy lies in equivocating on 'necessary'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim under attack
The trick denies that possibility (imkan) is a cause of need for an effect (علة للاحتياج إلى المؤثر). This is a common argument in kalam: the contingent needs a cause.
The trick aims to undermine this principle.
2
Extract the syllogism
The argument: Major premise: 'Everything that is a cause of need for an effect is necessarily a cause of need for an effect.' Minor premise: 'Nothing of possibility is necessarily a cause of need for an effect.' Conclusion: 'Therefore, no possibility is a cause of need for an effect.'
Note the use of 'necessarily' in the major and minor.
3
Check the validity
The syllogism appears valid in form (Celarent or something), but the issue is the meaning of 'necessarily'. The major premise uses 'necessarily' as a general qualifier, but the minor uses it to deny a property. The argument becomes an equivocation on 'necessary'.
The source text may be garbled, but the fallacy seems to be that the minor premise denies necessity of the property, not the property itself.
4
Recognize the refutation
The author likely refuted this by showing that possibility is indeed a cause in the sense of grounding the need for a cause, even if not necessarily in itself. The trick plays on the ambiguity.
The full refutation is not preserved, but the trick is a classic sophism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في النكت المشهورة في باب المغالطات عند السلف ]
وأما النكت المشهورة في باب المغالطات عند السلف فهي هذه: [أ] أما الأولى فأن يقال بعض الجسم لا يكون قابلا للعدم وإلا لكان كلما كان الشيء جسما كان قابلا للعدم؛ والتالي محال فإنه إذا صدق كان ينظم مع المقدمة الصادقة في نفس الأمر وهي قولنا كلما كان الشيء جسما وليس قابلا للعدم فهو جسم قياسًا منتجا من الشكل الأول كقولنا كلما كان الشيء جسما وليس قابلا للعدم فهو قابل للعدم؛ وإنه محال. [ب] وأما الثانية فإن يقال إن الفلك مركب من أجزاء غير متجزئة فإنه يصدق قولنا قد يكون إذا كان الفلك جسما فهو مركب من أجزاء غير متجزئة من الشكل الثالث؛ والأوسط مجموع طرفيه . ثم يقال لكنه جسم دائمًا فيلزم أن يكون مركبًا من أجزاء غير متجزئة. Fourth Dialectical Trick: Body Qua Body Cannot Exist
Fourth sophistical argument: body qua body non-existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy of self-referential paradox when arguing about the existence of universals.

Core Idea
Arguing that body qua body cannot exist confuses universal and particular.

The fourth trick argues that 'body qua body cannot exist' because if it exists, it must be either external (particular) or in the mind (also particular). But body qua body is supposed to be universal, so it cannot exist as particular. This is a classic paradox about universals. The fallacy lies in treating the universal as if it could exist separately.

Guided Path
1
Understand the argument's structure
The trick: 'The body insofar as it is body cannot exist. For if it exists, then either (a) it exists externally, then it is particular (since it can be pointed to); or (b) it exists in the mind, then it is also particular. But body qua body is universal, so it cannot be particular. Hence it cannot exist.'
This is a form of dilemma.
2
Identify the fallacy
The fallacy is that the argument presupposes that the universal (body qua body) must exist in the same way as particulars. But universals do not exist as separate entities; they exist as concepts in the mind or as shared properties. The argument falsely assumes that existence necessarily entails particularity.
The trick is a sophism against realism about universals.
3
Consider the refutation
The author likely responds that body qua body is a mental concept (ma'qul) that can exist both in the mind as universal and in external particulars as a shared nature. So the dilemma is false.
The text is incomplete, but the pattern is clear.
4
Generalize the fallacy
This fallacy involves ignoring the distinction between modes of existence (wujud dhihni vs wujud khariji). The proposition 'body exists' can be true in the mind even if not externally as a universal.
This is a common issue in ontology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Fallacy of Taking a Conditional Judgment as Unconditional
First part of the passage on conditional judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a judgment that is true under a condition is falsely applied without that condition.

Core Idea
A judgment's truth depends on its context; ignoring the condition leads to fallacy.

In this passage, the author warns that a judgment made under a specific condition becomes false when taken without that condition. For example, a statement valid only at a certain time or place is erroneously applied universally. This fallacy often appears in Islamic sciences when rulings are generalized without their qualifying conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition in the judgment
Look for words like 'if', 'when', or a specific state/time attached to the judgment in the Arabic text. Here, the author mentions 'شرط أو في حال أو في وقت' (a condition, state, or time).
2
Determine the error when the condition is removed
The fallacy occurs when the judgment is taken without that condition, i.e., applied in all times or in a different time without the original restriction. The author explicitly states 'فيؤخذ بدون ذلك الشرط'.
3
Recognize the fallacy in practice
Understand that such a mistake leads to invalid conclusions. For instance, in Islamic law, a ruling that applies only to a specific case (e.g., a contract under duress) cannot be generalized to all contracts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكم على موضوع شرط أو في حال أو في وقت فيؤخذ بدون ذلك الشرط مثلا أو في كل وقت أو في وقت آخر دون ذلك الوقت. وأما ما يكون كاذبا في جهة الحكم فمثل ما إذا أخذ بالعرض مكان ما بالذات كما يقال إن السقامنيا مبردة بالذات؛ وإنما هي بالعرض لإزالتها المسخّن بالذات وهو الصفراء على ما عرف. وكذلك إذا أخذ ما بالقوة مكان ما بالفعل أو بالعكس كما مرّ.
Judgment about a subject with a condition or state or time, then it is taken without that condition, for example, or in every time or in another time without that time. As for what is false in the aspect of judgment, it is like when the accidental is taken in place of the essential, as it is said that scammony is essentially cooling; but it is only accidentally cooling because it removes what is essentially heating, namely yellow bile, as known. Likewise when the potential is taken in place of the actual or vice versa, as has passed.
Source pages p. 601

Commentary
This passage discusses two types of fallacies in judgment: (1) taking a conditional judgment without its condition, and (2) taking accidental as essential or potential as actual. The author gives examples: Scammony is said to be cooling essentially, but it is only accidentally cooling because it removes the cause of heat (yellow bile). Similarly, taking potential for actual or vice versa is a fallacy. The passage warns against these errors in logical reasoning.

Key Terms
الحكم
شرط
وقت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Taking the judgment without that condition
B) Confusing accidental with essential
C) Taking potential as actual
D) Using a false premise

Key Takeaway
A judgment that depends on a condition must not be applied without that condition; doing so is a fallacy.

Fallacy of Confusing Accidental with Essential and Potential with Actual
Second part of the passage on accidental/essential and potential/actual fallacies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish accidental from essential properties and potential from actual states in arguments, and identify when these are wrongly exchanged.

Core Idea
Mistaking an accidental property for essential leads to false causal reasoning.

The author continues by discussing fallacies in the 'aspect of judgment' (جهة الحكم). He gives the example of scammony (a plant used in medicine) being said to be essentially cooling, when in reality it is only accidentally cooling because it removes the cause of heat. Similarly, confusing potential with actual, or vice versa, is a fallacy. These errors are common in natural philosophy and medicine, and you must learn to spot them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the accidental and essential in the example
In the scammony case, the author says it is said to be 'مبردة بالذات' (essentially cooling), but it is actually 'بالعرض' (accidentally) cooling because it removes 'المسخّن بالذات' (the essentially heating) which is yellow bile. So cooling is an accidental effect, not essential.
2
Understand the distinction between potential and actual
The author also mentions taking 'ما بالقوة' (potential) in place of 'ما بالفعل' (actual) or vice versa. This is a similar confusion: a thing may have a potential property that it does not yet have actually. For example, a seed is potentially a tree but not actually a tree.
3
Apply the lesson to detect these fallacies
When evaluating arguments, check whether a property is claimed as essential or accidental, and whether a state is actual or only potential. If the reasoning relies on misidentifying these, the argument is flawed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Judgment about a subject with a condition or state or time, then it is taken without that condition, for example, or in every time or in another time without that time. As for what is false in the aspect of judgment, it is like when the accidental is taken in place of the essential, as it is said that scammony is essentially cooling; but it is only accidentally cooling because it removes what is essentially heating, namely yellow bile, as known. Likewise when the potential is taken in place of the actual or vice versa, as has passed.

This passage discusses two types of fallacies in judgment: (1) taking a conditional judgment without its condition, and (2) taking accidental as essential or potential as actual. The author gives examples: Scammony is said to be cooling essentially, but it is only accidentally cooling because it removes the cause of heat (yellow bile). Similarly, taking potential for actual or vice versa is a fallacy. The passage warns against these errors in logical reasoning.

Checkpoint
What is the fallacy in the statement 'Scammony is essentially cooling' according to the passage?
A) Taking conditional as unconditional
B) Taking accidental as essential
C) Taking potential as actual
D) Taking part as whole

Key Takeaway
Accidental properties are not part of a thing's essence; confusing them leads to false causal claims in medicine and other sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze a common claim in Islamic medicine: 'Honey is heating.' Determine whether this property is essential or accidental based on the mechanism (e.g., honey may heat by increasing blood flow).
?
Identify a statement from a legal text where a potential state (e.g., 'a child has the potential to be a witness') is mistakenly treated as actual.

Sophistry and Fallacies

The First Fallacy: Body Not Susceptible to Non-Existence
First fallacy: a claim about body and non-existence leads to contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of the first fallacy and explain why it is invalid using first-figure syllogistic reasoning.

Core Idea
A false premise about bodies leads to a contradiction in the first figure.

The first well-known fallacy from the predecessors involves a claim about some body not being susceptible to non-existence. The author shows that this claim, when combined with a true premise, yields an impossible conclusion via a first-figure syllogism. Let's dissect this sophism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial claim
The fallacy begins with the claim: 'Some body is not susceptible to non-existence.' This is the assumption that the sophist tries to prove.
The phrase 'some body' (بعض الجسم) indicates a particular affirmative proposition.
2
Extract the hidden premise
The author then notes that if this were true, it would combine with a true premise: 'Whenever a thing is a body and is not susceptible to non-existence, it is a body.' This premise is obviously true but trivial.
This is a universal affirmative: all bodies that are not susceptible to non-existence are bodies.
3
Construct the syllogism
From the two premises, we get a first-figure syllogism: Major: 'All bodies that are not susceptible to non-existence are bodies.' Minor: 'Some body is not susceptible to non-existence.' Conclusion: 'Some body is a body' (trivial). But the author derives a different conclusion: 'Therefore, it is susceptible to non-existence.' Wait, that seems contradictory.
Actually, the author says the combination yields the conclusion that the body is susceptible to non-existence, which contradicts the original claim. Let's re-read carefully.
4
Identify the contradiction
The author states that the false claim 'some body is not susceptible to non-existence', if assumed true, leads to the conclusion (via the first figure) that 'some body is susceptible to non-existence'. This is a direct contradiction, showing the original claim must be false.
The fallacy is that the original claim cannot be true because it leads to its own negation. This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في النكت المشهورة في باب المغالطات عند السلف ] وأما النكت المشهورة في باب المغالطات عند السلف فهي هذه: [أ] أما الأولى فأن يقال بعض الجسم لا يكون قابلا للعدم وإلا لكان كلما كان الشيء جسما كان قابلا للعدم؛ والتالي محال فإنه إذا صدق كان ينظم مع المقدمة الصادقة في نفس الأمر وهي قولنا كلما كان الشيء جسما وليس قابلا للعدم فهو جسم قياسًا منتجا من الشكل الأول كقولنا كلما كان الشيء جسما وليس قابلا للعدم فهو قابل للعدم؛ وإنه محال. [ب] وأما الثانية فإن يقال إن الفلك مركب من أجزاء غير متجزئة فإنه يصدق قولنا قد يكون إذا كان الفلك جسما فهو مركب من أجزاء غير متجزئة من الشكل الثالث؛ والأوسط مجموع طرفيه . ثم يقال لكنه جسم دائمًا فيلزم أن يكون مركبًا من أجزاء غير متجزئة. [ت] وأما الثالثة فيقال الإمكان لا يكون علة للاحتياج إلى المؤثر؛ إذ كل ما هو علة للاحتياج إلى المؤثر فهو ضروري أن يكون علة للاحتياج إلى المؤثر ولا شيء من الإمكان بضروري أن يكون علة للاحتياج إلى المؤثر شيء من الإمكان بعلة للاحتياج إلى المؤثر. [ث] وأما الرابعة فيقال إن الجسم من حيث هو الجسم لا يمكن أن يكون موجودًا فإنه إذا كان موجودًا فإما أن يكون موجودًا في الخارج ؛ وإنه هو الجزئي حينئذ لما أنه يمكن أن يشار إليه إشارة حسية، وإما أن يكون موجودًا في الذهن؛ وإنه هو الجزئي أيضًا لما أنه يمكن أن يشار إليه إشارة عقلية. والجسم من حيث هو الجسم لا يمكن أن يكون جزئيا وهو عام يعم جميع الأجسام فلا يمكن أن يكون موجودًا إذن. وإذا لم يمكن أن يكون الجسم من حيث هو الجسم موجودًا فلا يمكن أن يكون هذا العام للجسماني موجودًا. [ج] وأما الخامسة فيقال حدوث العالم لا يكون مستلزما نقيض الواقع، وكل ما يكون مستلزما نقيض الواقع فهو واقع؛ أما الصغرى فلأنه إذا كان مستلزما كان منتفيًا بالضرورة، ويلزم منه أنه إذا كان متحققا فلا يكون مستلزما. وأما الكبرى فلأنه إذا لم يكن مستلزما كان واقعا وإلا لكان مستلزما بالضرورة. ولا يقال كيف يكون مستلزما لنقيض الواقع وهو بعينه نقيض الواقع؛ فإن نقيض الواقع أعم منه. [ح] وأما السادسة فيقال إنه إذا كان حادثا فحدوثه لا يخلو من أن يكون حادثا؛ وإنه محال؛ إذ الكلام في ذلك الحادث نحو الكلام في هذا، أو لا يكون حادثا؛ وإنه محال أيضًا فإنه يلزمه منه إما القدم وإما العدم: والكل باطل. [خ] وأما السابعة فيقال إن الشيء لا يمكن أن يكون واقعاً إلا أن يكون واقعا وذلك لأن وقوعه إما لذاته؛ وإنهما في الذات على السواء، وإما لغيره؛ وإنهما في النسبة إلى ذلك الغير على السواء فلا يمكن أن يكون هذا واقعا دون ذلك، وبالعكس. [د] وأما الثامنة فيقال اجتماع الضدين وهما [٣٥٠] الصدق والكذب في كلام واحد واقع ؛ وذلك لأنه إذا قال واحد إن تكلمت من بعد فذلك صادق ثمَّ قال كذبت، فهذا الكلام إن كان صادقا فالكلام الأول صادق وكاذب معًا، وإن كان كاذبًا فكذلك وهذا ظاهر. [ذ] وأما التاسعة فيقال إذا كان الشيء ممكن الوجود كان من اللوازم أن لا يكون ممكن الوجود؛ وهذا محال وإنما قلناه؛ وذلك لأنه إذا كان ممكن الوجود فوجوده وعدمهممكن لا محالة. ومن المحال أن يكون كذلك.
Chapter on the well-known points in the chapter of fallacies according to the predecessors. As for the well-known points in the chapter of fallacies according to the predecessors, they are these: [A] The first: that some body is not susceptible to non-existence, otherwise whenever a thing is a body it would be susceptible to non-existence; the consequent is impossible, for if it were true, it would combine with the true premise in itself, which is our statement 'whenever a thing is a body and is not susceptible to non-existence, it is a body' to produce a valid syllogism from the first figure, as our statement 'whenever a thing is a body and is not susceptible to non-existence, it is susceptible to non-existence'; and that is impossible. [B] The second: that one says the celestial sphere is composed of indivisible parts, for our statement would be true: 'It may be that if the celestial sphere is a body, then it is composed of indivisible parts' from the third figure; the middle term is the sum of its two extremes. Then it is said but it is always a body, so it follows that it is composed of indivisible parts. [C] The third: that possibility cannot be a cause of need for the determinant; for everything that is a cause of need for the determinant is necessary to be a cause of need for the determinant, and no possibility is necessary to be a cause of need for the determinant; therefore no possibility is a cause of need for the determinant. [D] The fourth: that body qua body cannot exist. For if it exists, it either exists in the external world — and then it is particular, since it can be pointed to by sensory indication — or it exists in the mind — and then it is also particular, since it can be pointed to by intellectual indication. But body qua body cannot be particular, as it is a universal comprising all bodies. Therefore it cannot exist. And if body qua body cannot exist, then this universal of body cannot exist. [E] The fifth: that the temporal origination of the world does not entail the opposite of reality, and whatever entails the opposite of reality is real. As for the minor premise, because if it entailed, it would be necessarily non-existent, and it follows that if it were realized, it would not entail. As for the major premise, because if it does not entail, it is real, otherwise it would necessarily entail. And it is not said how can it entail the opposite of reality while it is itself the opposite of reality; for the opposite of reality is more general than it. [F] The sixth: that if something is originated, its origination is either originated — which is impossible, since the discussion about that originated thing is similar to the discussion about this — or it is not originated — which is also impossible, for it would entail either eternity or non-existence; both are false. [G] The seventh: that a thing cannot be realized unless it is realized, and that is because its realization is either through itself — and both are equal in selfhood — or through another — and both are equal in relation to that other, so it is not possible that one is realized without the other, and vice versa. [H] The eighth: that the conjunction of two opposites, namely truth and falsehood, occurs in a single statement. That is because if someone says 'What I speak after this is true' then says 'I lied', then this statement: if it is true, then the first statement is both true and false together; and if it is false, likewise. And this is evident. [I] The ninth: that if a thing is possible of existence, then it is necessary that it is not possible of existence; and this is impossible and we only say it. That is because if it is possible of existence, then its existence and non-existence are both possible necessarily. And it is impossible for it to be so.
Source pages p. 601

Commentary
This passage enumerates nine well-known fallacies (al-nukat al-mashhura) used by earlier scholars. Each fallacy is presented with a brief argument showing its logical flaw. The first involves the claim that some body is not susceptible to non-existence, leading to a contradiction in the first figure. The second argues that the celestial sphere is composed of indivisible parts by a third figure syllogism. The third deals with possibility (imkan) not being a cause of need for a determinant. The fourth argues that body qua body cannot exist because it would be either particular in external or mental existence, but it is universal. The fifth concerns the origination of the world not entailing the opposite of reality. The sixth argues that if something is originated, its origination must either be originated (infinite regress) or not (eternity or non-existence). The seventh states that a thing cannot be realized unless it is realized, leading to a self-referential paradox. The eighth presents the liar paradox: truth and falsehood can coexist in one statement. The ninth argues that if something is possible, it is necessary that it is not possible (a contradiction). These fallacies are classic sophisms in dialectical debates.

Key Terms
المغالطات
النكت المشهورة
الشكل الأول

Key Takeaway
The first fallacy shows how a false premise can be reduced to absurdity by combining it with a trivial truth in a syllogism, but the derivation itself must be carefully examined.

Construct a similar reductio argument: Assume that some animal is not capable of motion. Then combine with the true premise 'Every animal that is not capable of motion is an animal.' Does this lead to a contradiction? Explain why.

The Second Fallacy: Celestial Sphere Composed of Indivisible Parts
Second fallacy: celestial sphere composed of indivisible parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third-figure syllogism used in the second fallacy and explain why the conclusion does not follow.

Core Idea
A third-figure syllogism is misused to claim the celestial sphere is composed of atoms.

The second fallacy attempts to prove that the celestial sphere (al-falak) is composed of indivisible parts (atoms). The author outlines a syllogism using the third figure, but the reasoning is flawed. Let's analyze it step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion sought
The sophist wants to prove that the celestial sphere is composed of indivisible parts (atoms). This is a physical claim about the structure of the heavens.
2
Extract the premises
The argument: 'It may be that if the celestial sphere is a body, then it is composed of indivisible parts.' This is a conditional premise. Then it is asserted: 'But it is always a body.' So the conclusion: 'Therefore, it is composed of indivisible parts.'
The premises are: (P1) It is possible that if the celestial sphere is a body, then it is composed of indivisible parts. (P2) The celestial sphere is a body. Conclusion: Therefore, it is composed of indivisible parts.
3
Analyze the logical form
This is a modus ponens from a conditional, but the conditional is qualified with 'it may be' (قد يكون). That means the conditional is not asserted as true but as possible. Thus, the inference is invalid: from 'possibly if A then B' and 'A', we cannot conclude 'B'.
The author says this is from the third figure. In the third figure, the middle term is the sum of the two extremes. But the given syllogism seems different.
4
Recognize the fallacy
The fallacy is that the conditional is not necessary, only possible. The argument confuses possibility with actuality. Hence, the conclusion does not necessarily follow.
The author mentions the third figure (الشكل الثالث) with the middle term being the sum of the two extremes. This likely refers to a categorical syllogism in the third figure, but the text is ambiguous.

Commentary
This passage enumerates nine well-known fallacies (al-nukat al-mashhura) used by earlier scholars. Each fallacy is presented with a brief argument showing its logical flaw.
