The Third Fallacy: Possibility and the Need for a Determinant
Third fallacy: possibility not a cause of need for determinant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the categorical syllogism in the third fallacy and explain why the conclusion about possibility and determinant is invalid.

Core Idea
Denying that possibility is a cause of need for a determinant is a logical error.

The third fallacy deals with the concept of possibility (imkan) and its relation to the need for a determinant (mu'aththir). The argument attempts to show that possibility cannot be a cause for needing a determinant, but the syllogism is flawed. Let's break it down.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The claim is: 'Possibility is not a cause of need for the determinant.' The argument tries to prove this via a syllogism.
2
Extract the premises
The premises: (P1) Everything that is a cause of need for a determinant is necessary to be a cause of need for a determinant. (P2) No possibility is necessary to be a cause of need for a determinant. Conclusion: Therefore, no possibility is a cause of need for a determinant.
This is a categorical syllogism in the first figure? Actually, it looks like a form of Camestres? Let's see: P1: All causes of need are necessary causes. P2: No possibility is a necessary cause. Conclusion: No possibility is a cause of need.
3
Analyze the logical structure
The syllogism is: Major term: 'cause of need for determinant', Middle term: 'necessary cause of need', Minor term: 'possibility'. The syllogism is: All C are N. No P is N. Therefore, no P is C. This is a valid syllogism (Camestres) if the terms are correct.
But is the first premise true? The argument assumes that if something is a cause of need, it must necessarily be a cause of need. That may be questionable.
4
Identify the fallacy
The fallacy is not in the form but in the premises. The first premise 'Everything that is a cause of need is necessarily a cause of need' is false. A cause of need might be conditional or contingent. The argument uses a false premise to derive a conclusion that may also be false.
Thus the fallacy is 'false premise' or 'begging the question'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في النكت المشهورة في باب المغالطات عند السلف ] وأما النكت المشهورة في باب المغالطات عند السلف فهي هذه: [أ] أما الأولى فأن يقال بعض الجسم لا يكون قابلا للعدم وإلا لكان كلما كان الشيء جسما كان قابلا للعدم؛ والتالي محال فإنه إذا صدق كان ينظم مع المقدمة الصادقة في نفس الأمر وهي قولنا كلما كان الشيء جسما وليس قابلا للعدم فهو جسم قياسًا منتجا من الشكل الأول كقولنا كلما كان الشيء جسما وليس قابلا للعدم فهو قابل للعدم؛ وإنه محال. [ب] وأما الثانية فإن يقال إن الفلك مركب من أجزاء غير متجزئة فإنه يصدق قولنا قد يكون إذا كان الفلك جسما فهو مركب من أجزاء غير متجزئة من الشكل الثالث؛ والأوسط مجموع طرفيه . ثم يقال لكنه جسم دائمًا فيلزم أن يكون مركبًا من أجزاء غير متجزئة. [ت] وأما الثالثة فيقال الإمكان لا يكون علة للاحتياج إلى المؤثر؛ إذ كل ما هو علة للاحتياج إلى المؤثر فهو ضروري أن يكون علة للاحتياج إلى المؤثر ولا شيء من الإمكان بضروري أن يكون علة للاحتياج إلى المؤثر شيء من الإمكان بعلة للاحتياج إلى المؤثر. [ث] وأما الرابعة فيقال إن الجسم من حيث هو الجسم لا يمكن أن يكون موجودًا فإنه إذا كان موجودًا فإما أن يكون موجودًا في الخارج ؛ وإنه هو الجزئي حينئذ لما أنه يمكن أن يشار إليه إشارة حسية، وإما أن يكون موجودًا في الذهن؛ وإنه هو الجزئي أيضًا لما أنه يمكن أن يشار إليه إشارة عقلية. والجسم من حيث هو الجسم لا يمكن أن يكون جزئيا وهو عام يعم جميع الأجسام فلا يمكن أن يكون موجودًا إذن. وإذا لم يمكن أن يكون الجسم من حيث هو الجسم موجودًا فلا يمكن أن يكون هذا العام للجسماني موجودًا. [ج] وأما الخامسة فيقال حدوث العالم لا يكون مستلزما نقيض الواقع، وكل ما يكون مستلزما نقيض الواقع فهو واقع؛ أما الصغرى فلأنه إذا كان مستلزما كان منتفيًا بالضرورة، ويلزم منه أنه إذا كان متحققا فلا يكون مستلزما. وأما الكبرى فلأنه إذا لم يكن مستلزما كان واقعا وإلا لكان مستلزما بالضرورة. ولا يقال كيف يكون مستلزما لنقيض الواقع وهو بعينه نقيض الواقع؛ فإن نقيض الواقع أعم منه. [ح] وأما السادسة فيقال إنه إذا كان حادثا فحدوثه لا يخلو من أن يكون حادثا؛ وإنه محال؛ إذ الكلام في ذلك الحادث نحو الكلام في هذا، أو لا يكون حادثا؛ وإنه محال أيضًا فإنه يلزمه منه إما القدم وإما العدم: والكل باطل. [خ] وأما السابعة فيقال إن الشيء لا يمكن أن يكون واقعاً إلا أن يكون واقعا وذلك لأن وقوعه إما لذاته؛ وإنهما في الذات على السواء، وإما لغيره؛ وإنهما في النسبة إلى ذلك الغير على السواء فلا يمكن أن يكون هذا واقعا دون ذلك، وبالعكس. [د] وأما الثامنة فيقال اجتماع الضدين وهما [٣٥٠] الصدق والكذب في كلام واحد واقع ؛ وذلك لأنه إذا قال واحد إن تكلمت من بعد فذلك صادق ثمَّ قال كذبت، فهذا الكلام إن كان صادقا فالكلام الأول صادق وكاذب معًا، وإن كان كاذبًا فكذلك وهذا ظاهر. [ذ] وأما التاسعة فيقال إذا كان الشيء ممكن الوجود كان من اللوازم أن لا يكون ممكن الوجود؛ وهذا محال وإنما قلناه؛ وذلك لأنه إذا كان ممكن الوجود فوجوده وعدمهممكن لا محالة. ومن المحال أن يكون كذلك.
Chapter on the well-known points in the chapter of fallacies according to the predecessors. As for the well-known points in the chapter of fallacies according to the predecessors, they are these: [A] The first: that some body is not susceptible to non-existence, otherwise whenever a thing is a body it would be susceptible to non-existence; the consequent is impossible, for if it were true, it would combine with the true premise in itself, which is our statement 'whenever a thing is a body and is not susceptible to non-existence, it is a body' to produce a valid syllogism from the first figure, as our statement 'whenever a thing is a body and is not susceptible to non-existence, it is susceptible to non-existence'; and that is impossible. [B] The second: that one says the celestial sphere is composed of indivisible parts, for our statement would be true: 'It may be that if the celestial sphere is a body, then it is composed of indivisible parts' from the third figure; the middle term is the sum of its two extremes. Then it is said but it is always a body, so it follows that it is composed of indivisible parts. [C] The third: that possibility cannot be a cause of need for the determinant; for everything that is a cause of need for the determinant is necessary to be a cause of need for the determinant, and no possibility is necessary to be a cause of need for the determinant; therefore no possibility is a cause of need for the determinant. [D] The fourth: that body qua body cannot exist. For if it exists, it either exists in the external world — and then it is particular, since it can be pointed to by sensory indication — or it exists in the mind — and then it is also particular, since it can be pointed to by intellectual indication. But body qua body cannot be particular, as it is a universal comprising all bodies. Therefore it cannot exist. And if body qua body cannot exist, then this universal of body cannot exist. [E] The fifth: that the temporal origination of the world does not entail the opposite of reality, and whatever entails the opposite of reality is real. As for the minor premise, because if it entailed, it would be necessarily non-existent, and it follows that if it were realized, it would not entail. As for the major premise, because if it does not entail, it is real, otherwise it would necessarily entail. And it is not said how can it entail the opposite of reality while it is itself the opposite of reality; for the opposite of reality is more general than it. [F] The sixth: that if something is originated, its origination is either originated — which is impossible, since the discussion about that originated thing is similar to the discussion about this — or it is not originated — which is also impossible, for it would entail either eternity or non-existence; both are false. [G] The seventh: that a thing cannot be realized unless it is realized, and that is because its realization is either through itself — and both are equal in selfhood — or through another — and both are equal in relation to that other, so it is not possible that one is realized without the other, and vice versa. [H] The eighth: that the conjunction of two opposites, namely truth and falsehood, occurs in a single statement. That is because if someone says 'What I speak after this is true' then says 'I lied', then this statement: if it is true, then the first statement is both true and false together; and if it is false, likewise. And this is evident. [I] The ninth: that if a thing is possible of existence, then it is necessary that it is not possible of existence; and this is impossible and we only say it. That is because if it is possible of existence, then its existence and non-existence are both possible necessarily. And it is impossible for it to be so.
Source pages p. 601

Commentary
This passage enumerates nine well-known fallacies (al-nukat al-mashhura) used by earlier scholars. Each fallacy is presented with a brief argument showing its logical flaw. The first involves the claim that some body is not susceptible to non-existence, leading to a contradiction in the first figure. The second argues that the celestial sphere is composed of indivisible parts by a third figure syllogism. The third deals with possibility (imkan) not being a cause of need for a determinant. The fourth argues that body qua body cannot exist because it would be either particular in external or mental existence, but it is universal. The fifth concerns the origination of the world not entailing the opposite of reality. The sixth argues that if something is originated, its origination must either be originated (infinite regress) or not (eternity or non-existence). The seventh states that a thing cannot be realized unless it is realized, leading to a self-referential paradox. The eighth presents the liar paradox: truth and falsehood can coexist in one statement. The ninth argues that if something is possible, it is necessary that it is not possible (a contradiction). These fallacies are classic sophisms in dialectical debates.

Key Terms
الإمكان
العلة
المؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the logical flaw in the third fallacy?
A) The minor premise is false
B) The syllogism is in an invalid mood
C) The major premise is false
D) The conclusion does not follow from the premises

Key Takeaway
The third fallacy shows that a valid syllogism can still be fallacious if it relies on a false premise, especially one involving necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar syllogism: 'Every cause of motion is necessarily a cause of motion. No push is necessarily a cause of motion. Therefore, no push is a cause of motion.' Is this valid? Which premise is questionable?
?
Explain why the major premise 'Everything that is a cause of need for the determinant is necessarily a cause of need' is false in the context of Islamic theology.

Sophistry and Fallacies
intermediate

The Fourth Fallacy: Body qua Body Cannot Exist
Fourth fallacy: body qua body cannot exist.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the argument that body qua body cannot exist and identify the fallacy concerning universals and particulars.

Core Idea
Confusing universal body with particular body leads to a false conclusion about existence.

The fourth fallacy argues that body as such (body qua body) cannot exist because it would have to be either external or mental, and both are particular, while body qua body is universal. This is a classic puzzle about universals. Let's examine the logic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the separation
The argument splits existence into external (fi al-kharij) and mental (fi al-dhihn). It claims that if body exists, it must be one of these, and both are particular (juz'i). But body qua body (min haythu huwa jism) is universal (kulli). Therefore, body qua body cannot exist.
2
Check the reasoning
The argument assumes that anything that exists must be particular. This is a key premise. But is it true that everything that exists is particular? In Islamic philosophy, universals exist in the mind as concepts, but also in external reality as properties of particulars. The term 'body qua body' refers to the essence, which exists in the mind as a universal.
3
Identify the fallacy
The fallacy lies in the hidden premise that existence entails particularity. While particular existence is one mode, universal concepts also have a kind of existence (mental existence). The argument equivocates between 'existence' in the concrete sense and 'existence' as a concept. Thus it is a fallacy of equivocation.

Source Text

Chapter on the well-known points in the chapter of fallacies according to the predecessors. As for the well-known points in the chapter of fallacies according to the predecessors, they are these: [A] The first: that some body is not susceptible to non-existence, otherwise whenever a thing is a body it would be susceptible to non-existence; the consequent is impossible, for if it were true, it would combine with the true premise in itself, which is our statement 'whenever a thing is a body and is not susceptible to non-existence, it is a body' to produce a valid syllogism from the first figure, as our statement 'whenever a thing is a body and is not susceptible to non-existence, it is susceptible to non-existence'; and that is impossible. [B] The second: that one says the celestial sphere is composed of indivisible parts, for our statement would be true: 'It may be that if the celestial sphere is a body, then it is composed of indivisible parts' from the third figure; the middle term is the sum of its two extremes. Then it is said but it is always a body, so it follows that it is composed of indivisible parts. [C] The third: that possibility cannot be a cause of need for the determinant; for everything that is a cause of need for the determinant is necessary to be a cause of need for the determinant, and no possibility is necessary to be a cause of need for the determinant; therefore no possibility is a cause of need for the determinant. [D] The fourth: that body qua body cannot exist. For if it exists, it either exists in the external world — and then it is particular, since it can be pointed to by sensory indication — or it exists in the mind — and then it is also particular, since it can be pointed to by intellectual indication. But body qua body cannot be particular, as it is a universal comprising all bodies. Therefore it cannot exist. And if body qua body cannot exist, then this universal of body cannot exist. [E] The fifth: that the temporal origination of the world does not entail the opposite of reality, and whatever entails the opposite of reality is real. As for the minor premise, because if it entailed, it would be necessarily non-existent, and it follows that if it were realized, it would not entail. As for the major premise, because if it does not entail, it is real, otherwise it would necessarily entail. And it is not said how can it entail the opposite of reality while it is itself the opposite of reality; for the opposite of reality is more general than it. [F] The sixth: that if something is originated, its origination is either originated — which is impossible, since the discussion about that originated thing is similar to the discussion about this — or it is not originated — which is also impossible, for it would entail either eternity or non-existence; both are false. [G] The seventh: that a thing cannot be realized unless it is realized, and that is because its realization is either through itself — and both are equal in selfhood — or through another — and both are equal in relation to that other, so it is not possible that one is realized without the other, and vice versa. [H] The eighth: that the conjunction of two opposites, namely truth and falsehood, occurs in a single statement. That is because if someone says 'What I speak after this is true' then says 'I lied', then this statement: if it is true, then the first statement is both true and false together; and if it is false, likewise. And this is evident. [I] The ninth: that if a thing is possible of existence, then it is necessary that it is not possible of existence; and this is impossible and we only say it. That is because if it is possible of existence, then its existence and non-existence are both possible necessarily. And it is impossible for it to be so.

Commentary
This passage enumerates nine well-known fallacies (al-nukat al-mashhura) used by earlier scholars. Each fallacy is presented with a brief argument showing its logical flaw. The first involves the claim that some body is not susceptible to non-existence, leading to a contradiction in the first figure. The second argues that the celestial sphere is composed of indivisible parts by a third figure syllogism. The third deals with possibility (imkan) not being a cause of need for a determinant. The fourth argues that body qua body cannot exist because it would be either particular in external or mental existence, but it is universal. The fifth concerns the origination of the world not entailing the opposite of reality. The sixth argues that if something is originated, its origination must either be originated (infinite regress) or not (eternity or non-existence). The seventh states that a thing cannot be realized unless it is realized, leading to a self-referential paradox. The eighth presents the liar paradox: truth and falsehood can coexist in one statement. The ninth argues that if something is possible, it is necessary that it is not possible (a contradiction). These fallacies are classic sophisms in dialectical debates.

The Fifth Fallacy: Huduth and Entailment of the Opposite of Reality
Fifth fallacy: huduth and entailment of opposite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to parse the conditional reasoning in the fifth fallacy and identify the fallacy of circular reasoning.

Core Idea
The fifth fallacy uses a self-referential conditional to argue about huduth and reality.

The fifth fallacy concerns the temporal origination of the world (huduth al-alam) and whether it entails the opposite of reality (naqid al-waqi'). The argument is convoluted, but it essentially tries to prove that huduth does not entail the opposite, using a conditional that refers to itself. Let's untangle it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
The claim is: 'The temporal origination of the world does not entail the opposite of reality.' This is the minor premise. The major premise: 'Whatever entails the opposite of reality is real.' Conclusion: 'Therefore, the temporal origination of the world is real.' But that is not the intended conclusion; rather it's to deny the entailment.
2
Examine the minor premise justification
The text says: 'if it entailed, it would be necessarily non-existent, and it follows that if it were realized, it would not entail.' This is obscure. It seems to argue that if huduth entailed the opposite, then huduth would be impossible (non-existent), and thus if it exists, it would not entail. This is circular.
3
Find the self-reference
The argument seems to define 'entailment of the opposite of reality' in terms of itself. The author tries to avoid the objection that the entailment is itself the opposite of reality, but the reasoning is confusing.
4
Identify the fallacy
The fallacy is one of 'begging the question' or circular definition. The argument assumes what it wants to prove: that huduth does not entail the opposite, and then uses that to derive a conclusion. The logical structure is invalid.

فصل : [ في النكت المشهورة في باب المغالطات عند السلف ] وأما النكت المشهورة في باب المغالطات عند السلف فهي هذه: [أ] أما الأولى فأن يقال بعض الجسم لا يكون قابلا للعدم وإلا لكان كلما كان الشيء جسما كان قابلا للعدم؛ والتالي محال فإنه إذا صدق كان ينظم مع المقدمة الصادقة في نفس الأمر وهي قولنا كلما كان الشيء جسما وليس قابلا للعدم فهو جسم قياسًا منتجا من الشكل الأول كقولنا كلما كان الشيء جسما وليس قابلا للعدم فهو قابل للعدم؛ وإنه محال. [ب] وأما الثانية فإن يقال إن الفلك مركب من أجزاء غير متجزئة فإنه يصدق قولنا قد يكون إذا كان الفلك جسما فهو مركب من أجزاء غير متجزئة من الشكل الثالث؛ والأوسط مجموع طرفيه . ثم يقال لكنه جسم دائمًا فيلزم أن يكون مركبًا من أجزاء غير متجزئة. [ت] وأما الثالثة فيقال الإمكان لا يكون علة للاحتياج إلى المؤثر؛ إذ كل ما هو علة للاحتياج إلى المؤثر فهو ضروري أن يكون علة للاحتياج إلى المؤثر ولا شيء من الإمكان بضروري أن يكون علة للاحتياج إلى المؤثر شيء من الإمكان بعلة للاحتياج إلى المؤثر. [ث] وأما الرابعة فيقال إن الجسم من حيث هو الجسم لا يمكن أن يكون موجودًا فإنه إذا كان موجودًا فإما أن يكون موجودًا في الخارج ؛ وإنه هو الجزئي حينئذ لما أنه يمكن أن يشار إليه إشارة حسية، وإما أن يكون موجودًا في الذهن؛ وإنه هو الجزئي أيضًا لما أنه يمكن أن يشار إليه إشارة عقلية. والجسم من حيث هو الجسم لا يمكن أن يكون جزئيا وهو عام يعم جميع الأجسام فلا يمكن أن يكون موجودًا إذن. وإذا لم يمكن أن يكون الجسم من حيث هو الجسم موجودًا فلا يمكن أن يكون هذا العام للجسماني موجودًا. [ج] وأما الخامسة فيقال حدوث العالم لا يكون مستلزما نقيض الواقع، وكل ما يكون مستلزما نقيض الواقع فهو واقع؛ أما الصغرى فلأنه إذا كان مستلزما كان منتفيًا بالضرورة، ويلزم منه أنه إذا كان متحققا فلا يكون مستلزما. وأما الكبرى فلأنه إذا لم يكن مستلزما كان واقعا وإلا لكان مستلزما بالضرورة. ولا يقال كيف يكون مستلزما لنقيض الواقع وهو بعينه نقيض الواقع؛ فإن نقيض الواقع أعم منه. [ح] وأما السادسة فيقال إنه إذا كان حادثا فحدوثه لا يخلو من أن يكون حادثا؛ وإنه محال؛ إذ الكلام في ذلك الحادث نحو الكلام في هذا، أو لا يكون حادثا؛ وإنه محال أيضًا فإنه يلزمه منه إما القدم وإما العدم: والكل باطل. [خ] وأما السابعة فيقال إن الشيء لا يمكن أن يكون واقعاً إلا أن يكون واقعا وذلك لأن وقوعه إما لذاته؛ وإنهما في الذات على السواء، وإما لغيره؛ وإنهما في النسبة إلى ذلك الغير على السواء فلا يمكن أن يكون هذا واقعا دون ذلك، وبالعكس. [د] وأما الثامنة فيقال اجتماع الضدين وهما [٣٥٠] الصدق والكذب في كلام واحد واقع ؛ وذلك لأنه إذا قال واحد إن تكلمت من بعد فذلك صادق ثمَّ قال كذبت، فهذا الكلام إن كان صادقا فالكلام الأول صادق وكاذب معًا، وإن كان كاذبًا فكذلك وهذا ظاهر. [ذ] وأما التاسعة فيقال إذا كان الشيء ممكن الوجود كان من اللوازم أن لا يكون ممكن الوجود؛ وهذا محال وإنما قلناه؛ وذلك لأنه إذا كان ممكن الوجود فوجوده وعدمهممكن لا محالة. ومن المحال أن يكون كذلك.
Chapter on the well-known points in the chapter of fallacies according to the predecessors. As for the well-known points in the chapter of fallacies according to the predecessors, they are these: [A] The first: that some body is not susceptible to non-existence, otherwise whenever a thing is a body it would be susceptible to non-existence; the consequent is impossible, for if it were true, it would combine with the true premise in itself, which is our statement 'whenever a thing is a body and is not susceptible to non-existence, it is a body' to produce a valid syllogism from the first figure, as our statement 'whenever a thing is a body and is not susceptible to non-existence, it is susceptible to non-existence'; and that is impossible. [B] The second: that one says the celestial sphere is composed of indivisible parts, for our statement would be true: 'It may be that if the celestial sphere is a body, then it is composed of indivisible parts' from the third figure; the middle term is the sum of its two extremes. Then it is said but it is always a body, so it follows that it is composed of indivisible parts. [C] The third: that possibility cannot be a cause of need for the determinant; for everything that is a cause of need for the determinant is necessary to be a cause of need for the determinant, and no possibility is necessary to be a cause of need for the determinant; therefore no possibility is a cause of need for the determinant. [D] The fourth: that body qua body cannot exist. For if it exists, it either exists in the external world — and then it is particular, since it can be pointed to by sensory indication — or it exists in the mind — and then it is also particular, since it can be pointed to by intellectual indication. But body qua body cannot be particular, as it is a universal comprising all bodies. Therefore it cannot exist. And if body qua body cannot exist, then this universal of body cannot exist. [E] The fifth: that the temporal origination of the world does not entail the opposite of reality, and whatever entails the opposite of reality is real. As for the minor premise, because if it entailed, it would be necessarily non-existent, and it follows that if it were realized, it would not entail. As for the major premise, because if it does not entail, it is real, otherwise it would necessarily entail. And it is not said how can it entail the opposite of reality while it is itself the opposite of reality; for the opposite of reality is more general than it. [F] The sixth: that if something is originated, its origination is either originated — which is impossible, since the discussion about that originated thing is similar to the discussion about this — or it is not originated — which is also impossible, for it would entail either eternity or non-existence; both are false. [G] The seventh: that a thing cannot be realized unless it is realized, and that is because its realization is either through itself — and both are equal in selfhood — or through another — and both are equal in relation to that other, so it is not possible that one is realized without the other, and vice versa. [H] The eighth: that the conjunction of two opposites, namely truth and falsehood, occurs in a single statement. That is because if someone says 'What I speak after this is true' then says 'I lied', then this statement: if it is true, then the first statement is both true and false together; and if it is false, likewise. And this is evident. [I] The ninth: that if a thing is possible of existence, then it is necessary that it is not possible of existence; and this is impossible and we only say it. That is because if it is possible of existence, then its existence and non-existence are both possible necessarily. And it is impossible for it to be so.
Source pages p. 601

Commentary
This passage enumerates nine well-known fallacies (al-nukat al-mashhura) used by earlier scholars. Each fallacy is presented with a brief argument showing its logical flaw. The first involves the claim that some body is not susceptible to non-existence, leading to a contradiction in the first figure. The second argues that the celestial sphere is composed of indivisible parts by a third figure syllogism. The third deals with possibility (imkan) not being a cause of need for a determinant. The fourth argues that body qua body cannot exist because it would be either particular in external or mental existence, but it is universal. The fifth concerns the origination of the world not entailing the opposite of reality. The sixth argues that if something is originated, its origination must either be originated (infinite regress) or not (eternity or non-existence). The seventh states that a thing cannot be realized unless it is realized, leading to a self-referential paradox. The eighth presents the liar paradox: truth and falsehood can coexist in one statement. The ninth argues that if something is possible, it is necessary that it is not possible (a contradiction). These fallacies are classic sophisms in dialectical debates.

Key Terms
الحدوث
نقيض الواقع
المستلزم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary logical error in the fifth fallacy?
A) Equivocation on 'reality'
B) Begging the question
C) Affirming the consequent
D) Denying the antecedent

Key Takeaway
The fifth fallacy illustrates that complex conditional arguments can hide circular reasoning, which must be exposed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Rephrase the fifth fallacy in your own words and identify the circular step.
?
Consider a simpler argument: 'If this sentence is true, then it is false. Therefore it is false.' This is a version of the liar paradox. Compare with the fifth fallacy.

Sophistry and Fallacies
intermediate
8 min
The Sixth Fallacy: The Origination of the Originated
Sixth fallacy: origination of the originated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the infinite regress in the sixth fallacy and explain why it is a fallacy.

Core Idea
Regress argument about the origination of an originated thing leads to a dead end.

The sixth fallacy considers an originated thing (hadith) and asks whether its origination itself is originated or not. Both options lead to problems: either an infinite regress or the thing becomes eternal or non-existent. This is a classic argument against infinite regress in causation.

Guided Path
1
Present the dilemma
If something is originated (hadith), then its origination (huduth) itself: either it is originated or it is not originated. If it is originated, then we have a regress (its origination also needs origination, ad infinitum). If it is not originated, then either it is eternal (qadim) or non-existent (ma'dum), both contradicting the fact that it is an origination.
2
Analyze the first horn
If the origination is also originated, then we need another origination for that origination, and so on infinitely. This is an infinite regress.
3
Analyze the second horn
If the origination is not originated, then it is either eternal or non-existent. But eternal origination is a oxymoron (origination implies a beginning), and non-existent cannot be the cause of existence. So both are impossible.
4
Identify the fallacy
The fallacy lies in the assumption that the origination event itself requires a further origination. In reality, we distinguish between the thing and its coming into being. The origination is not a separate entity that needs its own cause; it is simply the fact that the thing began to exist. The argument commits a category mistake.

The Seventh and Eighth Fallacies: Self-Reference and the Liar Paradox
Seventh and eighth fallacies: self-reference and liar paradox.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify self-referential paradoxes and explain why the liar paradox leads to a conjunction of truth and falsehood.

Core Idea
Self-referential statements can create paradoxes where truth and falsehood coincide.

The seventh and eighth fallacies are both about self-reference. The seventh argues that a thing cannot be realized unless it is already realized, leading to a regress. The eighth is the classic liar paradox: a statement that claims to be false, leading to a contradiction. Let's explore these two together.

Guided Path
1
Understand the seventh fallacy
It argues that something cannot become realized unless its realization is already realized. This leads to an infinite regress of realizations. The fallacy is that it assumes realization is a property that requires its own prior realization, like needing a cause.
2
Understand the eighth fallacy (liar paradox)
The liar paradox: If someone says 'What I speak after this is true' and then says 'I lied', then that statement: if it is true, then the first statement is true, but if the second statement is true (that he lied), then the first must be false, so it is both true and false. This is a clear contradiction.
3
Identify the common thread
Both involve self-reference. In the seventh, 'realization' refers to itself; in the eighth, the truth of a statement refers to itself. Self-reference leads to paradoxes if not handled carefully.
4
Recognize why they are fallacies
These are not arguments but puzzles that can be used to confuse. They are fallacies because they create contradictions that cannot be resolved within the same logical framework. In a serious debate, one must point out the self-reference and reject the premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في النكت المشهورة في باب المغالطات عند السلف ] وأما النكت المشهورة في باب المغالطات عند السلف فهي هذه: [أ] أما الأولى فأن يقال بعض الجسم لا يكون قابلا للعدم وإلا لكان كلما كان الشيء جسما كان قابلا للعدم؛ والتالي محال فإنه إذا صدق كان ينظم مع المقدمة الصادقة في نفس الأمر وهي قولنا كلما كان الشيء جسما وليس قابلا للعدم فهو جسم قياسًا منتجا من الشكل الأول كقولنا كلما كان الشيء جسما وليس قابلا للعدم فهو قابل للعدم؛ وإنه محال. [ب] وأما الثانية فإن يقال إن الفلك مركب من أجزاء غير متجزئة فإنه يصدق قولنا قد يكون إذا كان الفلك جسما فهو مركب من أجزاء غير متجزئة من الشكل الثالث؛ والأوسط مجموع طرفيه . ثم يقال لكنه جسم دائمًا فيلزم أن يكون مركبًا من أجزاء غير متجزئة. [ت] وأما الثالثة فيقال الإمكان لا يكون علة للاحتياج إلى المؤثر؛ إذ كل ما هو علة للاحتياج إلى المؤثر فهو ضروري أن يكون علة للاحتياج إلى المؤثر ولا شيء من الإمكان بضروري أن يكون علة للاحتياج إلى المؤثر شيء من الإمكان بعلة للاحتياج إلى المؤثر. [ث] وأما الرابعة فيقال إن الجسم من حيث هو الجسم لا يمكن أن يكون موجودًا فإنه إذا كان موجودًا فإما أن يكون موجودًا في الخارج ؛ وإنه هو الجزئي حينئذ لما أنه يمكن أن يشار إليه إشارة حسية، وإما أن يكون موجودًا في الذهن؛ وإنه هو الجزئي أيضًا لما أنه يمكن أن يشار إليه إشارة عقلية. والجسم من حيث هو الجسم لا يمكن أن يكون جزئيا وهو عام يعم جميع الأجسام فلا يمكن أن يكون موجودًا إذن. وإذا لم يمكن أن يكون الجسم من حيث هو الجسم موجودًا فلا يمكن أن يكون هذا العام للجسماني موجودًا. [ج] وأما الخامسة فيقال حدوث العالم لا يكون مستلزما نقيض الواقع، وكل ما يكون مستلزما نقيض الواقع فهو واقع؛ أما الصغرى فلأنه إذا كان مستلزما كان منتفيًا بالضرورة، ويلزم منه أنه إذا كان متحققا فلا يكون مستلزما. وأما الكبرى فلأنه إذا لم يكن مستلزما كان واقعا وإلا لكان مستلزما بالضرورة. ولا يقال كيف يكون مستلزما لنقيض الواقع وهو بعينه نقيض الواقع؛ فإن نقيض الواقع أعم منه. [ح] وأما السادسة فيقال إنه إذا كان حادثا فحدوثه لا يخلو من أن يكون حادثا؛ وإنه محال؛ إذ الكلام في ذلك الحادث نحو الكلام في هذا، أو لا يكون حادثا؛ وإنه محال أيضًا فإنه يلزمه منه إما القدم وإما العدم: والكل باطل. [خ] وأما السابعة فيقال إن الشيء لا يمكن أن يكون واقعاً إلا أن يكون واقعا وذلك لأن وقوعه إما لذاته؛ وإنهما في الذات على السواء، وإما لغيره؛ وإنهما في النسبة إلى ذلك الغير على السواء فلا يمكن أن يكون هذا واقعا دون ذلك، وبالعكس. [د] وأما الثامنة فيقال اجتماع الضدين وهما [٣٥٠] الصدق والكذب في كلام واحد واقع ؛ وذلك لأنه إذا قال واحد إن تكلمت من بعد فذلك صادق ثمَّ قال كذبت، فهذا الكلام إن كان صادقا فالكلام الأول صادق وكاذب معًا، وإن كان كاذبًا فكذلك وهذا ظاهر. [ذ] وأما التاسعة فيقال إذا كان الشيء ممكن الوجود كان من اللوازم أن لا يكون ممكن الوجود؛ وهذا محال وإنما قلناه؛ وذلك لأنه إذا كان ممكن الوجود فوجوده وعدمهممكن لا محالة. ومن المحال أن يكون كذلك.
Chapter on the well-known points in the chapter of fallacies according to the predecessors. As for the well-known points in the chapter of fallacies according to the predecessors, they are these: [A] The first: that some body is not susceptible to non-existence, otherwise whenever a thing is a body it would be susceptible to non-existence; the consequent is impossible, for if it were true, it would combine with the true premise in itself, which is our statement 'whenever a thing is a body and is not susceptible to non-existence, it is a body' to produce a valid syllogism from the first figure, as our statement 'whenever a thing is a body and is not susceptible to non-existence, it is susceptible to non-existence'; and that is impossible. [B] The second: that one says the celestial sphere is composed of indivisible parts, for our statement would be true: 'It may be that if the celestial sphere is a body, then it is composed of indivisible parts' from the third figure; the middle term is the sum of its two extremes. Then it is said but it is always a body, so it follows that it is composed of indivisible parts. [C] The third: that possibility cannot be a cause of need for the determinant; for everything that is a cause of need for the determinant is necessary to be a cause of need for the determinant, and no possibility is necessary to be a cause of need for the determinant; therefore no possibility is a cause of need for the determinant. [D] The fourth: that body qua body cannot exist. For if it exists, it either exists in the external world — and then it is particular, since it can be pointed to by sensory indication — or it exists in the mind — and then it is also particular, since it can be pointed to by intellectual indication. But body qua body cannot be particular, as it is a universal comprising all bodies. Therefore it cannot exist. And if body qua body cannot exist, then this universal of body cannot exist. [E] The fifth: that the temporal origination of the world does not entail the opposite of reality, and whatever entails the opposite of reality is real. As for the minor premise, because if it entailed, it would be necessarily non-existent, and it follows that if it were realized, it would not entail. As for the major premise, because if it does not entail, it is real, otherwise it would necessarily entail. And it is not said how can it entail the opposite of reality while it is itself the opposite of reality; for the opposite of reality is more general than it. [F] The sixth: that if something is originated, its origination is either originated — which is impossible, since the discussion about that originated thing is similar to the discussion about this — or it is not originated — which is also impossible, for it would entail either eternity or non-existence; both are false. [G] The seventh: that a thing cannot be realized unless it is realized, and that is because its realization is either through itself — and both are equal in selfhood — or through another — and both are equal in relation to that other, so it is not possible that one is realized without the other, and vice versa. [H] The eighth: that the conjunction of two opposites, namely truth and falsehood, occurs in a single statement. That is because if someone says 'What I speak after this is true' then says 'I lied', then this statement: if it is true, then the first statement is both true and false together; and if it is false, likewise. And this is evident. [I] The ninth: that if a thing is possible of existence, then it is necessary that it is not possible of existence; and this is impossible and we only say it. That is because if it is possible of existence, then its existence and non-existence are both possible necessarily. And it is impossible for it to be so.
Source pages p. 601

Commentary
This passage enumerates nine well-known fallacies (al-nukat al-mashhura) used by earlier scholars. Each fallacy is presented with a brief argument showing its logical flaw. The first involves the claim that some body is not susceptible to non-existence, leading to a contradiction in the first figure. The second argues that the celestial sphere is composed of indivisible parts by a third figure syllogism. The third deals with possibility (imkan) not being a cause of need for a determinant. The fourth argues that body qua body cannot exist because it would be either particular in external or mental existence, but it is universal. The fifth concerns the origination of the world not entailing the opposite of reality. The sixth argues that if something is originated, its origination must either be originated (infinite regress) or not (eternity or non-existence). The seventh states that a thing cannot be realized unless it is realized, leading to a self-referential paradox. The eighth presents the liar paradox: truth and falsehood can coexist in one statement. The ninth argues that if something is possible, it is necessary that it is not possible (a contradiction). These fallacies are classic sophisms in dialectical debates.

Key Terms
اجتماع الضدين
الصدق
الكذب
الواقع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the result of the liar paradox as described in the eighth fallacy?
A) The first statement is always true
B) The second statement is always false
C) Truth and falsehood coexist in the same statement
D) The speaker is lying

Key Takeaway
The seventh and eighth fallacies demonstrate that self-reference can lead to logical paradoxes that a sound argument must avoid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Create your own liar paradox: 'This sentence is false.' Is it true or false? Explain why it is a fallacy.
?
Consider a variant: 'All statements are false.' Does this include itself? Identify the problem.

Sophistry and Fallacies
advanced
8 min
The Ninth Fallacy: Possible Existence Implies Necessary Non-Possibility
Ninth fallacy: possible existence implies impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the modal fallacy in the ninth fallacy and explain why the argument is invalid.

Core Idea
A possible thing cannot be such that it is impossible, but the argument claims it does.

The ninth fallacy argues that if something is possible of existence, then it must be impossible. This is a contradiction that the author uses as a sophism. Let's analyze the modal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the claim
The argument says: 'If a thing is possible of existence, then it is necessary that it is not possible of existence.' That is, a possible thing is necessarily impossible. This is a direct contradiction.
2
Understand the justification
The justification: 'If it is possible of existence, then its existence and non-existence are both possible necessarily. And it is impossible for it to be so.' The idea is that if both existence and non-existence are possible, then in all cases it is contingent. But the conclusion that it is impossible does not follow.
3
Identify the modal error
The fallacy confuses possibility and necessity. From 'both existence and non-existence are possible' we cannot deduce that existence is impossible. In fact, possible means not impossible. The argument tries to derive impossibility from possibility, which is self-defeating.
4
Formalize the fallacy
Let P = 'thing is possible of existence'. The argument: P implies that both existence and non-existence are possible. Then it claims that this situation is impossible, so not-P. But that is a misunderstanding of modal logic.

Chapter on the well-known points in the chapter of fallacies according to the predecessors. As for the well-known points in the chapter of fallacies according to the predecessors, they are these: [A] The first: that some body is not susceptible to non-existence, otherwise whenever a thing is a body it would be susceptible to non-existence; the consequent is impossible, for if it were true, it would combine with the true premise in itself, which is our statement 'whenever a thing is a body and is not susceptible to non-existence, it is a body' to produce a valid syllogism from the first figure, as our statement 'whenever a thing is a body and is not susceptible to non-existence, it is susceptible to non-existence'; and that is impossible. Then it is said but it is always a body, so it follows that it is composed of indivisible parts. [C] The third: that possibility cannot be a cause of need for the determinant; for everything that is a cause of need for the determinant is necessary to be a cause of need for the determinant, and no possibility is necessary to be a cause of need for the determinant; therefore no possibility is a cause of need for the determinant. [D] The fourth: that body qua body cannot exist. For if it exists, it either exists in the external world — and then it is particular, since it can be pointed to by sensory indication — or it exists in the mind — and then it is also particular, since it can be pointed to by intellectual indication. But body qua body cannot be particular, as it is a universal comprising all bodies. Therefore it cannot exist. And if body qua body cannot exist, then this universal of body cannot exist. [E] The fifth: that the temporal origination of the world does not entail the opposite of reality, and whatever entails the opposite of reality is real. As for the major premise, because if it does not entail, it is real, otherwise it would necessarily entail. Commentary
This passage enumerates nine well-known fallacies (al-nukat al-mashhura) used by earlier scholars. Each fallacy is presented with a brief argument showing its logical flaw. The first involves the claim that some body is not susceptible to non-existence, leading to a contradiction in the first figure. The second argues that the celestial sphere is composed of indivisible parts by a third figure syllogism. The third deals with possibility (imkan) not being a cause of need for a determinant. The fourth argues that body qua body cannot exist because it would be either particular in external or mental existence, but it is universal. The fifth concerns the origination of the world not entailing the opposite of reality. The seventh states that a thing cannot be realized unless it is realized, leading to a self-referential paradox. The eighth presents the liar paradox: truth and falsehood can coexist in one statement. The ninth argues that if something is possible, it is necessary that it is not possible (a contradiction). These fallacies are classic sophisms in dialectical debates.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the ninth fallacy?
A) Everything that is possible must exist
B) Everything that is possible is impossible
C) Everything that is possible is necessary
D) Nothing is possible

Key Takeaway
The ninth fallacy shows the importance of distinguishing possibility from necessity; eliding them leads to contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar fallacy: 'If it is possible that it rains, then it is impossible that it does not rain. Therefore, rain is either necessary or impossible.' Identify the error.
?
Explain why the possibility of a thing does not undermine its existence; rather, it shows it is contingent.
